Causality and Created Things

The Four Elements: Composition Evidence from Plant Growth
States the theory and presents composition evidence from plant growth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observation of plant formation provides empirical evidence for the theory that all generated bodies are composed of the four elements.

Core Idea
Generation requires all four elements: earth, water, air, and heat.

This lesson introduces the philosophical theory that the four elements are the fundamental components of all generated bodies. The author presents a key piece of empirical evidence: the formation of plants from the combination of earth, water, air, and heat from the sun. This observation supports the claim that composition is only from these four.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the terms for the four elements and the description of plant formation.
The author uses the words أرض, ماء, هواء, and حرارة.
2
Identify the theory
The passage begins by stating that the philosophers made these four the pillars (أركان) of generated things. Notice the terms 'composition' (تركيب) and 'analysis' (تحليل).
The theory is that all compounds are composed of these four and decompose into them.
3
Analyze the composition evidence
Focus on the sentence starting with 'أما التركيب' which describes how plants occur when the four elements combine, and how animals eventually form from plants. Note the condition that if one element is missing generation fails.
This is presented as a witnessed observation (نشاهد).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ثم جعلوا هذه الأربعة أركان المواليد منها التركيب وإليها التحليل، لما يشاهد من أنه إذا اجتمع الأرض والماء مع تحلل الهواء، وإفاضة حرارة من السماء تكون النبات وصار مادة لتكون الحيوان، وأنه إذا وضع مركب في القرع والانبيق، تصاعد منه جزء هوائي وتقاطر مائي، وبقي أرضي ولا بد من الناري لتعين على حدوث الصورة الحافظة للتركيب الحاصل بالأسباب المقتضية، وضعفه ظاهر. يعني ذهب الفلاسفة إلى أن هذه العناصر أركان جميع المركبات العنصرية، أعني المسماة بالمواليد أعني المعادن والنبات والحيوان، بمعنى أن تركب جميعها، إنما هو من هذه الأربعة وتحليل جميعها، إنما هو إليها، أما التركيب فلأنا نشاهد أنه إذا اجتمع الماء والتراب مع تخلل الهواء وفيضان حرارة من الشمس حدث النبات، ثم إنه يصير غذاء للحيوان، فيتأدى بحسب ما يتوارد عليه من الاستحالات والانقلابات إلى أن يتكون منه حيوان، ولو فقد واحد من الأربعة لم يحدث كالتراب بلا رطوبة أو بلا هواء متخلل، أو حرارة طابخة، وأما التحليل فلأنا إذا وضعنا مركباً في القرع والانبيق، وأوقدنا عليه ناراً من شأنها تفريق المختلفات تصاعد منه أجزاء هوائية، وتقاطر أجزاء مائية، وبقي أجزاء أرضية. ومعلوم أنه لا بد من أجزاء نارية تفيد طبخاً ونضجاً، يوجب حصول مزاج يستتبع صورة نوعية حاصلة تحفظ الأجزاء المجتمعة بالأسباب السابقة عن التفرق والانفصال والمركب عن الانحلال، إذ ربما لا يكفي تلك الأسباب لكونها على التقضي والزوال، وفيما ذكرنا دفع لما يقال أن شأن الحرارة تفريق المختلفات، فكيف تكون جامعة لها؟ وأنه لا بد للتجاور والاجتماع بين الجزء
He said: Then they made these four the pillars of generated things [al-mawalid]; from them is composition and to them is analysis, based on what is observed: that when earth and water come together with the penetration of air and the pouring of heat from the sky, plants are formed and become matter for the formation of animals; and that when a compound is placed in a flask and alembic, a vaporous part ascends from it, a watery part distills, and an earthly part remains, and fire is necessary to assist in the occurrence of the form preserving the composition obtained through the efficient causes, and its weakness is apparent. Meaning, the philosophers went to the view that these elements are the pillars of all elemental compounds, i.e., what are called generated things, namely minerals, plants, and animals, in the sense that the composition of all of them is only from these four, and the analysis of all of them is only into them. As for composition, we observe that when water and soil come together with the penetration of air and the pouring of heat from the sun, plants occur, then they become food for animals, and it progresses according to the transformations and changes that occur until an animal is formed from it. If one of the four were missing, it would not occur, like soil without moisture, or without penetrating air, or scorching heat. As for analysis, we place a compound in a flask and alembic, and kindle a fire whose nature is to separate different things, then vaporous parts ascend from it, watery parts distill, and earthly parts remain. And it is known that fiery parts are necessary to provide cooking and ripening, which necessitates obtaining a temperament that entails a specific form that preserves the assembled parts, due to the previous causes, from dispersion and separation, and the compound from dissolution, for perhaps those causes are insufficient because they are transient and vanishing. In what we have mentioned is a refutation of what is said: the nature of heat is to separate different things, so how can it be a unifier? And that there must be for the contiguity and coming together of the part...
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage expounds the philosophical theory of the four elements as the ultimate components of all generated things (al-mawalid). It begins by stating the theory: all compound bodies are composed of these four elements and decompose into them. Evidence for composition is drawn from observation: when earth, water, air, and heat from the sky combine, plants form and later become matter for animals. Missing any element prevents generation. Evidence for analysis comes from distillation: a compound placed in a flask and alembic separates into vaporous, watery, and earthly parts, with fire necessary to produce the specific form that preserves the compound. The passage then addresses an objection: heat is known to separate things, so how can it unify? The answer is that fire provides cooking and ripening, which achieves a temperament that sustains the compound. The text ends mid-sentence, but the argument establishes the empirical basis for the four-element theory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what evidence is given for composition from the four elements?
A) Logical deduction from the nature of heat.
B) Observation of plant growth when earth, water, air, and heat combine.
C) The authority of ancient philosophers.
D) The Islamic doctrine of creation.

Key Takeaway
Empirical observation of plant growth from earth, water, air, and heat demonstrates that all generated bodies are composed of the four elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a plant growing, do you only see soil, water, air, and sunlight—or do you perceive the unseen power of Allah that brings life from these elements? Will you pause this week to thank the Creator for every sprout you see?
?
The philosophers argue that if one element is missing, generation fails. But what if the real missing element in your spiritual life is sincere reliance on Allah (tawakkul)? What step will you take today to restore that element?
?
This passage shows that even the philosophers rely on empirical observation. Yet you often follow inherited beliefs without examining their basis. What is one belief you hold about the natural world that you have never questioned, and how will you test it against revelation this week?

Evidence from Distillation and the Dual Role of Fire
Provides evidence from distillation and addresses the objection about heat's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how distillation provides evidence for the four-element theory and how fire's role as both separator and unifier is explained through the production of temperament and specific form.

Core Idea
Distillation reveals the four elements; fire unifies through cooking and ripening.

This lesson presents a second line of empirical evidence: the distillation of compounds. By heating a compound in a flask and alembic, we observe it separates into vaporous, watery, and earthy components. The passage also addresses a key objection — how can heat both separate and unify? The answer lies in fire's ability to produce a temperament that preserves the compound's form.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on distillation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the apparatus (قرع وانبيق) and the description of the separation process.
2
Understand the analysis evidence
The passage describes how heating a compound separates it into air-like (vapors), water-like (distillate), and earth-like (residue) parts. This shows the compound is composed of these elements.
The fire is said to separate different things (تفريق المختلفات).
3
Examine the objection and answer
The author anticipates an objection: if fire separates, how can it also be a unifier? The answer is that fire provides cooking and ripening, which produces a temperament (مزاج) that gives rise to a specific form (صورة نوعية) that holds the compound together.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that this refutes the objection. Consider how the dual role of fire is essential for preserving composition.
The text breaks off but the argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ثم جعلوا هذه الأربعة أركان المواليد منها التركيب وإليها التحليل، لما يشاهد من أنه إذا اجتمع الأرض والماء مع تحلل الهواء، وإفاضة حرارة من السماء تكون النبات وصار مادة لتكون الحيوان، وأنه إذا وضع مركب في القرع والانبيق، تصاعد منه جزء هوائي وتقاطر مائي، وبقي أرضي ولا بد من الناري لتعين على حدوث الصورة الحافظة للتركيب الحاصل بالأسباب المقتضية، وضعفه ظاهر. يعني ذهب الفلاسفة إلى أن هذه العناصر أركان جميع المركبات العنصرية، أعني المسماة بالمواليد أعني المعادن والنبات والحيوان، بمعنى أن تركب جميعها، إنما هو من هذه الأربعة وتحليل جميعها، إنما هو إليها، أما التركيب فلأنا نشاهد أنه إذا اجتمع الماء والتراب مع تخلل الهواء وفيضان حرارة من الشمس حدث النبات، ثم إنه يصير غذاء للحيوان، فيتأدى بحسب ما يتوارد عليه من الاستحالات والانقلابات إلى أن يتكون منه حيوان، ولو فقد واحد من الأربعة لم يحدث كالتراب بلا رطوبة أو بلا هواء متخلل، أو حرارة طابخة، وأما التحليل فلأنا إذا وضعنا مركباً في القرع والانبيق، وأوقدنا عليه ناراً من شأنها تفريق المختلفات تصاعد منه أجزاء هوائية، وتقاطر أجزاء مائية، وبقي أجزاء أرضية. ومعلوم أنه لا بد من أجزاء نارية تفيد طبخاً ونضجاً، يوجب حصول مزاج يستتبع صورة نوعية حاصلة تحفظ الأجزاء المجتمعة بالأسباب السابقة عن التفرق والانفصال والمركب عن الانحلال، إذ ربما لا يكفي تلك الأسباب لكونها على التقضي والزوال، وفيما ذكرنا دفع لما يقال أن شأن الحرارة تفريق المختلفات، فكيف تكون جامعة لها؟ وأنه لا بد للتجاور والاجتماع بين الجزء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heat only separates things and cannot act as a unifying force.
Correction: Heat also provides cooking and ripening that produces a temperament and specific form, which preserves the compound and unifies its parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does fire contribute to preserving a compound during distillation?
A) It completely separates all components.
B) It provides cooking and ripening that yields a temperament giving rise to a specific form that preserves the compound.
C) It destroys the compound.
D) It only produces vapors.

Key Takeaway
Distillation shows that compounds contain the four elements, and fire plays a dual role: separating and also unifying through the production of a preserving temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced something that felt purely destructive—like the separating fire—only to later realize it was also ripening and unifying you? What was that, and how did it shape you?
?
In what area of your life might you be seeing only the 'fire's separation'—loss, pain, or change—without trusting that Allah is also cooking and ripening your character through it? What would change if you looked for His unifying purpose today?
?
This lesson shows that even in a physical process, fire has a preserving role by Allah's design. Do you believe that the 'fires' you face are under His control and intended to preserve your faith? How would you live differently if you truly held that conviction?

The Philosophers' Doctrine and Proof from Composition
Philosophers' elemental claim and composition proof from plant growth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that all natural bodies are composed of four elements and the observational proof from plant growth.

Core Idea
Four elements are the principles of all generated things, proven by observation.

The author begins by summarizing the philosophers' elemental theory, then elaborates with a gloss. The first proof is from composition: we see plants emerging from earth, water, air, and solar heat, forming the matter for animals. This observation suggests that all composite bodies arise from these four.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from a summary to an expanded explanation.
2
Identify the four elements
Find the four elements named: earth, water, air, and fire (implied as solar heat). Note that fire's role is highlighted.
3
Understand the composition proof
The proof is empirical: when earth, water, air, and solar heat come together, plants arise. Plants then become animal food, leading to animal generation.
This shows a chain from elements to higher organisms.
4
Note the caveat
The author stresses that if any one of the four is missing, generation fails. This emphasizes their necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا اجتمع الأرض والماء مع تحلل الهواء، وإفاضة حرارة من السماء تكون النبات وصار مادة لتكون الحيوان، وأنه إذا وضع مركب في القرع والانبيق، تصاعد منه جزء هوائي وتقاطر مائي، وبقي أرضي ولا بد من الناري لتعين على حدوث الصورة الحافظة للتركيب الحاصل بالأسباب المقتضية، وضعفه ظاهر. يعني ذهب الفلاسفة إلى أن هذه العناصر أركان جميع المركبات العنصرية أعني المسماة بالمواليد أعني المعادن والنبات والحيوان، بمعنى أن تركب جميعها، إنما هو من هذه الأربعة وتحليل جميعها، إنما هو إليها، أما التركيب فلأنا نشاهد أنه إذا اجتمع الماء والتراب مع تخلل الهواء وفيضان حرارة من الشمس حدث النبات، ثم إنه يصير غذاء للحيوان، فيتأدى بحسب ما يتوارد عليه من الاستحالات والانقلابات إلى أن يتكون منه حيوان، ولو فقد واحد من الأربعة لم يحدث كالتراب بلا رطوبة أو بلا هواء متخلل، أو حرارة طابخة، وأما التحليل فلأنا إذا وضعنا مركباً في القرع والانبيق، وأوقدنا عليه ناراً من شأنها تفريق المختلفات تصاعد منه أجزاء هوائية، وتقاطر أجزاء مائية، وبقي أجزاء أرضية. ومعلوم أنه لا بد من أجزاء نارية تفيد طبخاً ونضجاً، يوجب حصول مزاج يستتبع صورة نوعية حاصلة تحفظ الأجزاء المجتمعة بالأسباب السابقة عن التفرق والانفصال والمركب عن الانحلال، إذ ربما لا يكفي تلك الأسباب لكونها على التقضي والزوال، وفيما ذكرنا دفع لما يقال أن شأن الحرارة تفريق المختلفات، فكيف تكون جامعة لها؟ وأنه لا بد للتجاور والاجتماع بين الجزء الناري وغيره من سبب يستديمه ريثما يتم التأثير والتأثير، فلم لا يكون هو المانع من تفريق الأجزاء من غير افتقار إلى الصورة النوعية.
When earth and water come together with the dissolution of air and the pouring of heat from the sky, plants are produced and become matter for the production of animals; and that when a compound is placed in a gourd (qarʿ) and alembic (anbīq), an airy part ascends, a watery part distills, and an earthy part remains, and a fiery part is necessary to assist in the occurrence of the form preserving the composition obtained by the requisite causes, and its weakness is apparent. He means: the philosophers held that these elements are the pillars of all elemental compounds – I mean those called generated things (mawālīd), namely minerals, plants, and animals – meaning that the composition of all of them is only from these four, and the analysis of all of them is only into them. As for composition, we observe that when water and earth come together with the permeation of air and the effusion of heat from the sun, plants occur; then it becomes food for animals, and leads, according to the transformations and changes it undergoes, to the formation of an animal from it. And if any one of the four were missing, it would not occur, like earth without moisture, or without permeating air, or a heating heat. As for analysis, when we place a compound in a gourd and alembic, and kindle a fire that is wont to separate different things, airy parts ascend, watery parts distill, and earthy parts remain. And it is known that fiery parts are necessary to provide cooking and ripening, which necessitate the occurrence of a temperament that entails a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) that preserves the parts collected by the previous causes from dispersion and separation, and the compound from dissolution, since perhaps those causes are not sufficient due to their being transient and perishable. And in what we have mentioned is a refutation of what is said: that the nature of heat is to separate different things, so how can it be a unifier? And that there must be for the adjacency and conjunction between the fiery part and others a cause that sustains it until the effect and impression are completed, so why would it not be the preventer of the separation of the parts without needing the specific form?
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' doctrine that the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) are the principles of all composite bodies, and it gives two observational proofs. The author first summarizes the philosophers' view in a gloss, then elaborates. The proof from composition: we observe that plants arise from earth, water, air, and solar heat; they then become animal food and eventually generate animals; if any element is missing, generation fails. The proof from analysis: when a compound is distilled in a gourd and alembic, airy parts ascend, watery parts distill, and earthy parts remain; fire is needed for cooking to produce a temperament that gives a specific form preserving the compound. The author then includes a refutation of an objection: heat is seen as a separator, so how can it unite? The response is that fire also provides a cause for adjacency and cohesion, preventing separation without needing a special form. This refutation is part of the philosophers' defense.

Key Terms
العناصر (al-ʿanāṣir)
المركبات (al-murakkabāt)
التركيب (al-tarkīb)
المواليد (al-mawālīd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the composition proof, what happens if any one of the four elements is missing?
A) Generation still occurs but is incomplete.
B) Generation cannot occur at all.
C) The resulting body lacks a specific form.
D) The missing element is replaced by another.

Key Takeaway
The composition proof demonstrates the necessity of the four elements for generation of plants and animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You look at plants every day—their colors, their growth. Do you see them as evidence of a Creator's wisdom, or do you take them for granted as mere natural products? What would change if you genuinely saw each plant as a sign pointing to something greater?
?
The philosophers based their worldview on observation and induction. But this lesson shows that their proof is not certain. On what basis do you hold your most fundamental beliefs? Are you willing to admit that some of them might be only probable? If so, what does that demand of you in seeking certainty?
?
When you eat food derived from plants, do you reflect that this simple act depends on the intricate arrangement of earth, water, air, and heat—all of which are sustained by Allah every moment? How often do you thank Him for the constant provision these elements represent?

Proof from Analysis and the Role of Fire
Analysis proof by distillation and refutation of heat objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' proof from chemical analysis (distillation) and their response to the objection that heat separates rather than unites.

Core Idea
Distillation reveals the elements, and fire's cooking role explains unity despite its separating tendency.

The second proof is from analysis: distillation of a compound yields airy, watery, and earthy parts, with fire playing an essential cooking role. The author then addresses a common objection—if heat separates, how can it unite?—by explaining that fire's cooking produces a temperament that sustains the compound's form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the distillation procedure and the subsequent objection-and-answer.
2
Analyze the distillation proof
The proof: when a compound is heated in a gourd and alembic, parts separate: airy (ascend), watery (distill), earthy (remain). Fire is needed for cooking to produce a temperament that gives a specific form preserving the compound.
3
Identify the objection
The objection: heat is known to separate different things, so how can it be the cause of unity? The passage presents this as a challenge.
4
Understand the refutation
The refutation: fire's cooking (ripening) produces a temperament that necessitates a specific form, which then preserves the parts from dispersion. Heat separates, but the form (from cooking) unites.
The philosophers argue that fire has both separating and uniting roles.

إذا اجتمع الأرض والماء مع تحلل الهواء، وإفاضة حرارة من السماء تكون النبات وصار مادة لتكون الحيوان، وأنه إذا وضع مركب في القرع والانبيق، تصاعد منه جزء هوائي وتقاطر مائي، وبقي أرضي ولا بد من الناري لتعين على حدوث الصورة الحافظة للتركيب الحاصل بالأسباب المقتضية، وضعفه ظاهر. يعني ذهب الفلاسفة إلى أن هذه العناصر أركان جميع المركبات العنصرية أعني المسماة بالمواليد أعني المعادن والنبات والحيوان، بمعنى أن تركب جميعها، إنما هو من هذه الأربعة وتحليل جميعها، إنما هو إليها، أما التركيب فلأنا نشاهد أنه إذا اجتمع الماء والتراب مع تخلل الهواء وفيضان حرارة من الشمس حدث النبات، ثم إنه يصير غذاء للحيوان، فيتأدى بحسب ما يتوارد عليه من الاستحالات والانقلابات إلى أن يتكون منه حيوان، ولو فقد واحد من الأربعة لم يحدث كالتراب بلا رطوبة أو بلا هواء متخلل، أو حرارة طابخة، وأما التحليل فلأنا إذا وضعنا مركباً في القرع والانبيق، وأوقدنا عليه ناراً من شأنها تفريق المختلفات تصاعد منه أجزاء هوائية، وتقاطر أجزاء مائية، وبقي أجزاء أرضية. ومعلوم أنه لا بد من أجزاء نارية تفيد طبخاً ونضجاً، يوجب حصول مزاج يستتبع صورة نوعية حاصلة تحفظ الأجزاء المجتمعة بالأسباب السابقة عن التفرق والانفصال والمركب عن الانحلال، إذ ربما لا يكفي تلك الأسباب لكونها على التقضي والزوال، وفيما ذكرنا دفع لما يقال أن شأن الحرارة تفريق المختلفات، فكيف تكون جامعة لها؟ وأنه لا بد للتجاور والاجتماع بين الجزء الناري وغيره من سبب يستديمه ريثما يتم التأثير والتأثير، فلم لا يكون هو المانع من تفريق الأجزاء من غير افتقار إلى الصورة النوعية.
When earth and water come together with the dissolution of air and the pouring of heat from the sky, plants are produced and become matter for the production of animals; and that when a compound is placed in a gourd (qarʿ) and alembic (anbīq), an airy part ascends, a watery part distills, and an earthy part remains, and a fiery part is necessary to assist in the occurrence of the form preserving the composition obtained by the requisite causes, and its weakness is apparent. He means: the philosophers held that these elements are the pillars of all elemental compounds – I mean those called generated things (mawālīd), namely minerals, plants, and animals – meaning that the composition of all of them is only from these four, and the analysis of all of them is only into them. As for composition, we observe that when water and earth come together with the permeation of air and the effusion of heat from the sun, plants occur; then it becomes food for animals, and leads, according to the transformations and changes it undergoes, to the formation of an animal from it. And if any one of the four were missing, it would not occur, like earth without moisture, or without permeating air, or a heating heat. As for analysis, when we place a compound in a gourd and alembic, and kindle a fire that is wont to separate different things, airy parts ascend, watery parts distill, and earthy parts remain. And it is known that fiery parts are necessary to provide cooking and ripening, which necessitate the occurrence of a temperament that entails a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) that preserves the parts collected by the previous causes from dispersion and separation, and the compound from dissolution, since perhaps those causes are not sufficient due to their being transient and perishable. And in what we have mentioned is a refutation of what is said: that the nature of heat is to separate different things, so how can it be a unifier? And that there must be for the adjacency and conjunction between the fiery part and others a cause that sustains it until the effect and impression are completed, so why would it not be the preventer of the separation of the parts without needing the specific form?
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' doctrine that the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) are the principles of all composite bodies, and it gives two observational proofs. The author first summarizes the philosophers' view in a gloss, then elaborates. The proof from composition: we observe that plants arise from earth, water, air, and solar heat; they then become animal food and eventually generate animals; if any element is missing, generation fails. The proof from analysis: when a compound is distilled in a gourd and alembic, airy parts ascend, watery parts distill, and earthy parts remain; fire is needed for cooking to produce a temperament that gives a specific form preserving the compound. The author then includes a refutation of an objection: heat is seen as a separator, so how can it unite? The response is that fire also provides a cause for adjacency and cohesion, preventing separation without needing a special form. This refutation is part of the philosophers' defense.

القرع والانبيق (al-qarʿ wa al-anbīq)
الصورة النوعية (al-ṣūra al-nawʿiyya)
الأسباب (al-asbāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heat only separates different things; it cannot be a cause of unity.
Correction: Although heat separates, it also cooks to produce a temperament that yields a specific form, which unites and preserves the compound.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the specific form (al-ṣūra al-nawʿiyya)?

B) To preserve the collected parts from dispersion and the compound from dissolution.

D) To replace the need for elemental causes.

Key Takeaway
The analysis proof, combined with the refutation of the heat objection, shows that fire both separates and, through cooking, enables a unifying form.

?

Further Objections and the Author's Final Response
Author raises objections and concedes limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific objections raised against the necessity of fire and the limitation of the elemental theory's universality.

Core Idea
Even if observed, elemental theory is not proven universal.

In this passage, the author presents two additional objections to the necessity of fire and the universality of elemental composition. He then concedes that even if the argument holds for observed compounds, it does not extend to all compounds. This lesson unpacks these points and their implications for causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its English translation. Notice the structure: two objections followed by a concessive statement.
The passage begins with 'نعم يرد' signalling an objection.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection denies the necessity of a fiery part for cooking/ripening; the second challenges the preserver being a form rather than divine will or mixture.
These are alternative explanations to the philosophers' theory.
3
Understand the final concession
The author concedes that even if the philosophers' proof works for observed cases, it does not prove universality. Ask yourself why this is a significant limitation.
This concession weakens the entire argument from composition.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this passage illustrates the author's critical approach: he raises plausible alternatives and then points out the argument's limited scope.

Commentary
This passage presents further objections to the philosophers' argument and a final concession. The first objection: the cooking and ripening attributed to fire could instead be caused by the heat of airy parts or heat from celestial bodies, so fire is not necessary. The second objection: the preservation of the compound could be due to the will of God (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār) or simply the mixture of moist and dry, not requiring a specific form. Finally, the author concedes that even if the philosophers' proofs for composition and analysis are accepted for observed cases, that does not prove that all compounds—including those not observed—are composed of the four elements. Thus the argument is not universal.

Objection on the Necessity of Fire for Cooking and Preservation
The author objects to the necessity of a specific fire for cooking and preservation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the necessity of a specific fiery substance for cooking and preservation, and appreciate the alternative possibilities raised by the author.

Core Idea
Fire is not the only possible cause; divine will or mere mixture could suffice.

In this passage, the author raises a powerful objection against the claim that a specific fiery part is necessary for cooking and preservation. He suggests that other causes such as celestial heat or God's direct will could achieve the same results. This objection challenges the assumption of necessary causal links in natural processes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author questions the necessity of a fiery part for cooking and preservation.
The objection is presented as a series of rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the alternative causes
List the three alternatives the author offers: (1) heat of airy parts or celestial bodies, (2) pure will of God, (3) mere mixture of wet and dry.
These alternatives challenge the standard view.
3
Reflect on the scope of the objection
Consider the author's concession: even if this reasoning holds for observed cases, it does not prove that all composites are like that. This limits the argument's scope.
This shows careful reasoning, not wholesale denial of causality.

The Transformation of Adjacent Elements: Definition and Examples
Definition and examples of element transformation into adjacent elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the four elements transforms into its adjacent counterpart through the processes of generation and corruption, and you will be able to identify the six direct transformations and the role of intermediaries for non-adjacent elements.

Core Idea
Elements change into neighbors by shedding one form and donning another.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces the concept of element transformation, which is called generation (kawm) and corruption (fasad). He begins by defining the process and then provides concrete examples from everyday phenomena, such as boiling water and liquefying stones. The lesson will help you grasp the fundamental idea that elements are not static but can transmute into their immediate neighbors via a change in form.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the content. Pay attention to the key terms: كَوْن (generation) and فَسَاد (corruption).
The phrase 'بخلع صورة، ولبس أخرى' is a vivid image of exchanging forms.
2
Identify the definition and the six types
The author defines transformation as taking off one form and putting on another. Then he lists three examples, each for an adjacent pair, and says that these make six types (since each pair can transform in both directions). Reflect on why there are six.
The six types come from the three adjacent pairs (fire-air, air-water, water-earth) each with two directions.
3
Consider non-adjacent transformations
The passage also mentions transformations between non-adjacent elements (e.g., fire to water) which require one or two intermediaries. Think about how this fits into the overall scheme.
For example, fire to water goes through air and then water (two intermediaries) or through air and water? Actually fire->air->water is one intermediary? Let's check: non-adjacent fire and water have air and water? Wait, the order is fire, air, water, earth. Non-adjacent pairs: fire-water (skip air? Actually fire and water are not adjacent because air is between them; fire and earth skip both air and water). The text says: 'four with one intermediary and two with two intermediaries'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني قال (المبحث الثاني) كل من الأربعة ينقلب إلى المجاور بخلع صورة، ولبس أخرى، ويسمى الكون والفساد، ففيما بين النار والهواء ظاهر، وفيما بين الهواء والماء، كما في غليان الماء وحصول القطرات على الإناء المكبوب على الجسد، وفيما بين الماء والأرض، كما في تسييل الأحجار بالجبل، وانعقاد مياه بعض العيون أحجاراً، فهذه ستة أنواع، وإذا اعتبر فيما بين غير المتجاورين جعلت أربعة بواسطة، واثنين بواسطتين . زعموا أن هيولي العناصر مشتركة قابلة لصورها النوعية، وخصوصيات الصور، إنما هي بحسب الاستعدادات الحاصلة بالأسباب الخارجة، فعند تبدل الأسباب الخارجة، والاستعدادات يجوز أن تزول صورة، وهو المراد بالفساد وتحدث صورة أخرى وهو المراد بالكون. وهذا معنى انقلاب عنصر إلى آخر، وقد علم أن النار فوق الكل، وتحتها الهواء ثم الماء ثم الأرض، وكل من الأربعة
The second investigation. He said (the second investigation): Each of the four [elements] changes into the adjacent one by taking off a form and putting on another, and it is called generation (kawm) and corruption (fasad). So regarding between fire and air it is apparent, and regarding between air and water, as in the boiling of water and the formation of droplets on the vessel inverted over the body, and regarding between water and earth, as in the liquefaction of stones by fire and the congealing of waters of some springs into stones. These are six types. And if considered between non-adjacent [elements], they become four with one intermediary and two with two intermediaries. They claimed that the hyle (matter) of the elements is common, receptive to their specific forms, and the particularities of the forms are only according to the dispositions (isti'dadat) arising from external causes. So when the external causes and the dispositions change, a form may cease—that is what is meant by corruption (fasad)—and another form may occur—that is what is meant by generation (kawm). And this is the meaning of the transformation of an element into another. And it is known that fire is above everything, and below it is air, then water, then earth, and each of the four...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage constitutes the second investigation (المبحث الثاني) of the chapter. It defines the transformation of the four classical elements (fire, air, water, earth) into each other through the processes of generation (الكون) and corruption (الفساد). The author states that each element changes into the one adjacent by shedding one form and acquiring another. Examples are given: between fire and air is obvious; between air and water as in water boiling and forming droplets on a lid; between water and earth as in the liquefaction of stones by fire (melted rock) and the solidification of spring water into stone (travertine). Six types of direct transformations are enumerated for adjacent pairs. For non-adjacent elements (e.g., fire to water), there are intermediaries (four with one intermediary, two with two intermediaries). The theoretical basis is then explained: there is a common hyle (prime matter) that can receive various specific forms; the particularity of these forms is due to dispositions (استعدادات) produced by external causes. When those external causes and dispositions change, one form disappears (فساد) and another appears (كون), which is the meaning of transmutation. The natural order places fire at the top, then air, then water, then earth at the bottom.

Key Terms
كون
فساد
نار
هواء
ماء
أرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All elements can transform directly into any other element.
Correction: According to this passage, direct transformation occurs only between adjacent elements (fire-air, air-water, water-earth). Transformations between non-adjacent elements require intermediaries.

Checkpoint
How many direct transformations are there between adjacent elements?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Two
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Each element transforms directly only into its neighbor by exchanging forms, yielding six types of direct change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it mean for you to transform into your 'neighbor'—to shed one form and put on another? What specific loss (corruption) and gain (generation) would that require? The passage describes water boiling into vapor and then condensing into droplets. Think of a recent pressure in your life—a 'boiling' situation. How did you respond? Did you rise as steam (escape) or eventually settle into a new state? What form did you shed, and what new form emerged? Is there an aspect of your character that needs to pass through a similar cycle of corruption and generation? What intermediary steps (like the intermediates between elements) might help you get there?

The Hyle and Dispositions: The Theory of Kawm and Fasad
Theoretical explanation of element transformation via hyle and dispositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theoretical basis of element transformation: a common hyle (prime matter) that receives specific forms depending on dispositions produced by external causes, explaining how generation and corruption occur.

Core Idea
A common matter underlies all elements, and forms change based on dispositions from external causes.

After defining the transformations, Sanusi delves into the underlying theory. He explains that there is a shared matter (hyle) that can take on different forms, and the particularity of each form is due to dispositions (isti'dadat) arising from external causes. This lesson unpacks that causal mechanism and also notes the natural order of the elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the terms: هيولي (hyle), استعداد (disposition), أسباب خارجية (external causes).
The theory is based on Aristotle's concept of prime matter.
2
Understand the role of hyle
The author claims that the hyle of the elements is common and receptive to all specific forms. This means there is a single underlying substance that can become fire, air, water, or earth.
This is a key metaphysical commitment in Islamic natural philosophy.
3
Examine the cause of form change
The particularities of each form are due to dispositions that arise from external causes. When these causes and dispositions change, one form vanishes (fasad) and another appears (kawm). This is the mechanism of transformation.
The process is not random but causal.
4
Note the natural order of elements
The passage concludes with the known order: fire is above everything, then air, then water, then earth. This hierarchy is related to their relative positions and transformations.
This order explains why transformations occur between adjacent elements in this sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني قال (المبحث الثاني) كل من الأربعة ينقلب إلى المجاور بخلع صورة، ولبس أخرى، ويسمى الكون والفساد، ففيما بين النار والهواء ظاهر، وفيما بين الهواء والماء، كما في غليان الماء وحصول القطرات على الإناء المكبوب على الجسد، وفيما بين الماء والأرض، كما في تسييل الأحجار بالجبل، وانعقاد مياه بعض العيون أحجاراً، فهذه ستة أنواع، وإذا اعتبر فيما بين غير المتجاورين جعلت أربعة بواسطة، واثنين بواسطتين . زعموا أن هيولي العناصر مشتركة قابلة لصورها النوعية، وخصوصيات الصور، إنما هي بحسب الاستعدادات الحاصلة بالأسباب الخارجة، فعند تبدل الأسباب الخارجة، والاستعدادات يجوز أن تزول صورة، وهو المراد بالفساد وتحدث صورة أخرى وهو المراد بالكون. وهذا معنى انقلاب عنصر إلى آخر، وقد علم أن النار فوق الكل، وتحتها الهواء ثم الماء ثم الأرض، وكل من الأربعة
The second investigation. He said (the second investigation): Each of the four [elements] changes into the adjacent one by taking off a form and putting on another, and it is called generation (kawm) and corruption (fasad). So regarding between fire and air it is apparent, and regarding between air and water, as in the boiling of water and the formation of droplets on the vessel inverted over the body, and regarding between water and earth, as in the liquefaction of stones by fire and the congealing of waters of some springs into stones. These are six types. And if considered between non-adjacent [elements], they become four with one intermediary and two with two intermediaries. They claimed that the hyle (matter) of the elements is common, receptive to their specific forms, and the particularities of the forms are only according to the dispositions (isti'dadat) arising from external causes. So when the external causes and the dispositions change, a form may cease—that is what is meant by corruption (fasad)—and another form may occur—that is what is meant by generation (kawm). And this is the meaning of the transformation of an element into another. And it is known that fire is above everything, and below it is air, then water, then earth, and each of the four...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage constitutes the second investigation (المبحث الثاني) of the chapter. It defines the transformation of the four classical elements (fire, air, water, earth) into each other through the processes of generation (الكون) and corruption (الفساد). The author states that each element changes into the one adjacent by shedding one form and acquiring another. Examples are given: between fire and air is obvious; between air and water as in water boiling and forming droplets on a lid; between water and earth as in the liquefaction of stones by fire (melted rock) and the solidification of spring water into stone (travertine). Six types of direct transformations are enumerated for adjacent pairs. For non-adjacent elements (e.g., fire to water), there are intermediaries (four with one intermediary, two with two intermediaries). The theoretical basis is then explained: there is a common hyle (prime matter) that can receive various specific forms; the particularity of these forms is due to dispositions (استعدادات) produced by external causes. When those external causes and dispositions change, one form disappears (فساد) and another appears (كون), which is the meaning of transmutation. The natural order places fire at the top, then air, then water, then earth at the bottom.

Key Terms

صورة نوعية
استعداد
أسباب خارجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forms are inherent in the matter and do not change.
Correction: The passage explains that forms are not inherent in the matter but arise from dispositions caused by external factors, and they can change when those factors change.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what causes the specific form of an element?
A) The hyle itself
B) The dispositions arising from external causes
C) The element's natural place
D) The element's temperature

Key Takeaway
Element transformation is explained by a common hyle and changing dispositions, providing a causal theory behind generation and corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
According to this passage, your character—like the elements—is not fixed. It changes based on external causes and the dispositions they produce. What external causes are you deliberately placing yourself under to shape your inner state? If you are not actively choosing them, who or what is choosing for you?
?
The theory says transformation occurs only between adjacent elements: fire to air, air to water, etc. In your spiritual life, are you trying to jump from a state of 'earth' (lowest) directly to 'fire' (highest) without passing through the intermediate stages? What causes are needed for gradual, adjacent transformation?
?
If the hyle of your heart is common to both iman and kufr, and only the dispositions from external causes determine which takes hold, then consider: what are the external causes you are exposing your heart to daily? Are you taking full responsibility for them, or are you passive? Answer honestly: what one external cause will you remove or add today to change a disposition that is corrupting you?

Elemental Transformations: Definition and Enumeration
Definition of kawm/fasad and enumeration of twelve elemental transformations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of elemental transformation (inqilāb al-ʿanāṣir), its basis in generation (kawm) and corruption (fasad), and how twelve types are derived from the four elements.

Core Idea
Elements transform into adjacent ones by exchanging forms, yielding twelve possible transformations.

This passage lays the foundation for understanding how the physical world changes at the elemental level. The author defines generation and corruption as the replacement of one form with another, then systematically enumerates all possible transformations between the four classical elements. The enumeration follows a logical hierarchy based on adjacency and intermediaries, not randomness.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the definitions of kawm and fasad as removal and acquisition of forms.
The passage begins with the matn: 'each of the four changes into the adjacent one...'
2
Identify the hierarchy and adjacency
Note the order: fire (top), air, water, earth (bottom). Adjacent pairs are fire-air, air-water, water-earth. This hierarchy is key to the enumeration.
Non-adjacent pairs require intermediaries.
3
Comprehend the theoretical basis
The author explains that the common hyle (hayula) is receptive to forms, and changes occur due to aptitudes (isti'dadat) from external causes. This justifies why transformation is possible.
This stems from Aristotelian hylomorphism.
4
Derive the twelve types mathematically
Work through the pairings: 3 adjacent pairs, 2 with one intermediary, 1 with two intermediaries. Each pairing yields two transformations (A→B and B→A). Total = (3+2+1)×2 = 12. Verify with the formula 4×3=12.
The passage explicitly states the total as twelve.
5
Note the empirical claim and exception
The author states that all transformations are empirically observed except air into water, which introduces the next topic. Reflect on why this might be less evident.
This shows the author's reliance on empirical testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
قال (المبحث الثاني) كل من الأربعة ينقلب إلى المجاور بخلع صورة، ولبس أخرى، ويسمى الكون (1) والفساد، ففيما بين النار والهواء ظاهر، وفيما بين الهواء والماء، كما في غليان الماء وحصول القطرات على الإناء المكبوب على الجسد، وفيما بين الماء والأرض، كما في تسييل الأحجار بالجبل، وانعقاد مياه بعض العيون أحجاراً، فهذه ستة أنواع، وإذا اعتبر فيما بين غير المتجاورين جعلت أربعة بواسطة، واثنين بواسطتين.
زعموا أن هيولي العناصر مشتركة قابلة لصورها النوعية، وخصوصيات الصور، إنما هي بحسب الاستعدادات الحاصلة بالأسباب الخارجة، فعند تبدل الأسباب الخارجة (2)، والاستعدادات يجوز أن تزول صورة، وهو المراد بالفساد وتحدث صورة (3) أخرى وهو المراد بالكون. وهذا معنى انقلاب عنصر إلى آخر، وقد علم أن النار فوق الكل، وتحتها الهواء ثم الماء ثم الأرض، وكل من الأربعة ينقلب إلى ما يجاوره، فتقع ثلاثة ازدواجات. أحدها بين النار والهواء، والثاني بين الهواء والماء، والثالث بين الماء والأرض، وإلى غير المجاور بواسطة واحدة فيقع ازدواجان أحدهما بين النار والماء والثاني بين الهواء والأرض، أو بواسطتين فيقع ازدواج واحد هو بين النار والأرض، ويشتمل كل ازدواج على نوعين من الكون والفساد، أعني انقلاب هذا إلى ذلك. وبالعكس. فالأنواع الأولية ستة . والتي بوسط أربعة، وبوسطين اثنان فالجميع اثنا عشر حاصلة ضرب كل من الأربعة في الثلاثة الباقية، ويشهد بوقوع الكل الحس والتجربة، ولم يقع الاشتباه إلا في انقلاب الهواء ماء.
The second section: He said: 'The second section: Each of the four [elements] changes into the adjacent one by removing a form and putting on another, and this is called generation (al-kawn) and corruption (al-fasad). So between fire and air it is apparent, and between air and water, as in the boiling of water and the appearance of droplets on the vessel inverted over the body, and between water and earth, as in the liquefaction of stones by the mountain and the congealing of waters of some springs into stones. So these are six types. And if one considers between non-adjacent [elements], they become four by one intermediary and two by two intermediaries.' They claimed that the hyle (hayula) of the elements is common, receptive to their specific forms, and the particularities of the forms are only according to the aptitudes (isti'dadat) resulting from external causes. So when the external causes and aptitudes change, it is possible that a form vanishes—that is what is meant by corruption—and another form occurs—that is what is meant by generation. And this is the meaning of the transformation of one element into another. And it is known that fire is above all, and beneath it is air, then water, then earth. And each of the four changes into what is adjacent to it, so three pairings occur: one between fire and air, the second between air and water, and the third between water and earth. And into the non-adjacent by one intermediary, two pairings occur: one between fire and water, the second between air and earth; or by two intermediaries, one pairing occurs, which is between fire and earth. And each pairing includes two types of generation and corruption—namely, the change of this into that, and vice versa. So the primary types are six, those by one intermediary are four, and by two intermediaries are two, so the total is twelve, obtained by multiplying each of the four by the remaining three. And sense and experience testify to the occurrence of all, and doubt has not occurred except concerning the transformation of air into water.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of elemental transformation (inqilāb al-ʿanāṣir) and enumerates the possible types. The author begins by quoting the matn: each of the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) transforms into the adjacent element by shedding one form and acquiring another, called generation (al-kawn) and corruption (al-fasad). Examples are given: fire-to-air is apparent; air-to-water is seen when water droplets appear on a vessel inverted over a hot body; water-to-earth is seen when stones liquefy or water congeals into stone. These give six types between adjacent pairs. For non-adjacent pairs, there are four types with one intermediary (e.g., fire-to-water via air) and two with two intermediaries (fire-to-earth via air and water).
The sharh then explains the theoretical basis: the common hyle (hayūlā) of elements is receptive to specific forms, and the forms change due to aptitudes (istiʿdādāt) from external causes. When causes and aptitudes change, a form is replaced—corruption and generation. The hierarchy is fire highest, then air, water, earth. From this, three adjacent pairings, two with one intermediary, one with two intermediaries. Each pairing yields two transformations (A to B and B to A), so total types: six primary, four with one intermediary, two with two intermediaries, summing to twelve (4 elements × 3 others). The author concludes that all are empirically verified except the transformation of air into water, which introduces the next topic. Note the key technical terms: kawm (generation), fasād (corruption), hayūlā (prime matter), istiʿdād (aptitude), zawāj (pair), and the hierarchical order of elements.

Refuting the Doubt: Perspiration and Vapor Explanations
Refutation of two alternative explanations for water droplets on a cooled vessel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two alternative explanations for the appearance of water on a cooled vessel and how the author refutes them, defending the doctrine of air-to-water transformation.

Core Idea
Empirical claims require eliminating alternative explanations to be established.

In this passage, the author tackles a scientific objection to the claim that air can transform into water. The objection notes that the water droplets on a cooled vessel could be explained by perspiration from the vessel or by condensation of pre-existing vapors. The author systematically dismantles both alternatives, showing they cannot account for the observed phenomena. This lesson walks through the doubt and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two alternative explanations (perspiration and vapor attraction) and the refutations.
The passage is dense with argument; take your time.
2
Understand the first refutation against perspiration
The author gives three reasons: hot water would be more likely to cause perspiration, dew only occurs in places of perspiration, and perspiration only makes sense if the vessel is filled with ice from the inside, not inverted.
This refutation shows that the perspiration explanation is inconsistent with observed patterns.
3
Understand the second refutation against vapor attraction
The author argues that fine watery particles would evaporate in hot air, they would deplete over time, descent from far would take longer, and descent is straight, not to the sides.
These points show the vapor explanation also fails.
4
Appreciate the method
The author defends a causal claim by eliminating alternative explanations, a classic scientific method.
This is an example of argument by elimination in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد قيل: إن ركوب القطرات على الإناء المبرد بالجمد، يجوز أن يكون للرشح أو لانجذاب الأبخرة إليه على ما قال أبو البركات أن في الهواء المطيف بالإناء أجزاء لطيفة مائية، لكنها لصغرها وجذب حرارة الهواء إياها لم تتمكن من خرق الهواء والنزول على الإناء ، فلما زالت سخونتها لمجاورة الإناء المبرد بالجمد كثفت وثقلت فنزلت واجتمعت على الإناء . وردّ الأول . بأنه لو كان للرشح لكان الماء الحار أولى بذلك لكونه ألطف ، ولما كان الندى إلا في مواضع الرشح ، على أن الرشح إنما يتوهم في الإناء المملوء بالجمد دون المكبوب عليه . والثاني بأنه لا يتصور بقاء هذا القدر من الأجزاء المائية في الهواء الحار الصيفي، بل لا بد من أن يتبخر ويتصعد. ولو سلّم فينبغي أن ينفد أو ينقص بالنزول، فلا تعود قطرات الإناء بعد إزالتها، ولو ادعى أنها نزلت من مسافة أبعد لزم أن تكون في زمان أطول، والوجود بخلافه على أن النزول إنما يكون على خط مستقيم فكيف يقع على جوانب الإناء .
For it was said: The appearance of droplets on the vessel cooled by ice could be due to perspiration or to the attraction of vapors to it, as Abu al-Barakat said: In the air surrounding the vessel there are fine watery particles, but due to their smallness and the heat of the air attracting them, they were unable to penetrate the air and descend upon the vessel. Then when their heat was removed by contact with the vessel cooled by ice, they condensed and became heavy, so they descended and collected on the vessel. And he refuted the first [explanation]: Because if it were due to perspiration, hot water would be more apt to do that because it is finer, and dew would only occur in places of perspiration, and furthermore, perspiration is only imagined in a vessel filled with ice, not in one inverted over it. And [he refuted] the second [explanation]: Because it is inconceivable that this quantity of watery particles could remain in the hot summer air; rather, they must evaporate and rise. And even if conceded, they should become depleted or decrease with descent, so the droplets on the vessel would not return after being removed. And if one claims that they descended from a farther distance, it would necessarily take a longer time, but experience shows otherwise. Moreover, descent occurs in a straight line; so how would it occur on the sides of the vessel?
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a doubt regarding the transformation of air into water, introduced earlier as the only questionable case. The doubt is presented: the appearance of droplets on a vessel cooled by ice could be explained either by perspiration (rashḥ) from the vessel or by the attraction of vapors (jadhb al-abkhira) from the surrounding air, which condense upon contact with the cold vessel, as argued by Abu al-Barakat. The author refutes both explanations.
First refutation (against perspiration): If it were perspiration, hot water would be more likely to produce it because hot water is finer. Moreover, dew only appears in places of perspiration, and perspiration is only plausible if the vessel is filled with ice from the inside, not if it is inverted over the body (so the cold is external).
Second refutation (against vapor attraction): It is impossible for fine watery particles to survive in the hot summer air; they would evaporate and rise. Furthermore, even if one concedes that they exist, they should diminish with each descent, so the droplets would not reappear after removal. Also, if they descended from a greater distance, the time would be longer, but experience contradicts this. Additionally, descent is in a straight line, so how would droplets gather on the sides of the vessel? The author thus defends the claim that air-to-water transformation is empirically valid by eliminating alternative explanations.

انجذاب
أبخرة
كثف
تبخر
انقلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The appearance of water on a cooled vessel is due to perspiration or vapor condensation, thus not evidence for air-to-water transformation.
Correction: The author systematically refutes both alternatives, showing they are untenable, thereby supporting the claim that it is genuine air-to-water transformation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason the author gives to refute the perspiration explanation?
A) Hot water would be more likely to cause perspiration.
B) Dew only occurs in places of perspiration.
C) Perspiration is only imagined in a vessel filled with ice, not inverted.
D) The droplets appear on all sides of the vessel, not just the bottom.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the empirical claim of air-to-water transformation by eliminating two plausible alternative explanations through logical and observational arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, do you ever challenge yourself to genuinely consider the strongest counterarguments, or do you avoid them? How can you train yourself to not shy away from doubts that might refine your faith?
?
The author did not dismiss the objector's explanations with a simple 'they are wrong'; he engaged each point carefully. In your personal relationships or intellectual pursuits, do you take the time to understand opposing views before responding? What would change if you did?
?
This lesson demands rigorous honesty in examining evidence. Are there areas in your life where you have accepted an easy explanation—about yourself, others, or your deen—without testing it against reality? What is one assumption you are ready to re-examine this week?

The Twelve Types of Elemental Transformation
Enumeration of elemental transformations into twelve types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the systematic classification of elemental transformations into twelve types based on adjacency and intermediaries.

Core Idea
Elemental transformations follow a precise mathematical pattern: 4 elements × 3 others = 12 types.

Before delving into specific cases, Sanusi provides a comprehensive overview of all possible elemental transformations. By considering whether elements are adjacent or separated by one or two intermediaries, he arrives at exactly twelve types. This classification is grounded in sensory experience, setting the stage for the later defense of air-to-water transformation, which is the only one doubted.

Guided Path
1
Explore the pairings of adjacent elements
Notice how fire-air, air-water, water-earth form three direct pairings. Each pairing involves two transformations (A to B and B to A).
2
Calculate the total number of types
Count the primary types (6), those with one intermediary (4), and those with two intermediaries (2), summing to 12. Understand the multiplication principle: 4 elements × 3 others.
This shows the system's logical completeness.
3
Observe the empirical claim
Sanusi asserts that all these transformations are witnessed by sensory experience and experiment, except for the transformation of air into water, which he will defend.
This leads to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينقلب إلى ما يجاوره ، فتقع ثلاثة ازدواجات . أحدها بين النار والهواء، والثاني بين الهواء والماء، والثالث بين الماء والأرض، وإلى غير المجاور بواسطة واحدة فيقع ازدواجـان أحدهما بين النار والماء والثاني بين الهواء والأرض، أو بواسطتين فيقع ازدواج واحد هو بين النار والأرض، ويشتمل كل ازدواج على نوعين من الكون والفساد، أعني انقلاب هذا إلى ذلك. وبالعكس. فالأنواع الأولية ستة . والتي بوسط أربعة، وبوسطين اثنان فالجميع اثنا عشر حاصلة ضرب كل من الأربعة في الثلاثة الباقية، ويشهد بوقوع الكل الحس والتجربة، ولم يقع الاشتباه إلا في انقلاب الهواء ماء . فقد قيل : إن ركوب القطرات على الإناء المبرد بالجمد، يجوز أن يكون للرشح أو لانجذاب الأبخرة إليه على ما قال أبو البركات أن في الهواء المطيف بالإناء أجزاء لطيفة مائية، لكنها لصغرها وجذب حرارة الهواء إياها لم تتمكن من خرق الهواء والنزول على الإناء ، فلما زالت سخونتها لمجاورة الإناء المبرد بالجمد كثفت وثقلت فنزلت واجتمعت على الإناء . وردّ الأول . بأنه لو كان للرشح لكان الماء الحار أولى بذلك لكونه ألطف ، ولما كان الندى إلا في مواضع الرشح ، على أن الرشح إنما يتوهم في الإناء المملوء بالجمد دون المكبوب عليه . والثاني بأنه لا يتصور بقاء هذا القدر من الأجزاء المائية في الهواء الحار الصيفي، بل لا بد من أن يتبخر ويتصعد. . ولو سلّم فينبغي أن ينفد أو ينقص بالنزول، فلا تعود قطرات الإناء بعد إزالتها، ولو ادعى أنها نزلت من مسافة أبعد لزم أن تكون في زمان أطول، والوجود بخلافه على أن النزول إنما يكون على خط مستقيم فكيف يقع على جوانب الإناء .
It transforms into what is adjacent to it, so three pairings occur. One is between fire and air, the second between air and water, and the third between water and earth. And to the non-adjacent via one intermediary, two pairings occur: one between fire and water, and the second between air and earth; or via two intermediaries, one pairing occurs, which is between fire and earth. Each pairing includes two types of generation and corruption (al-kawn wa al-fasad), namely the transformation of this into that, and vice versa. So the primary types are six, those with one intermediary are four, and those with two intermediaries are two, so the total is twelve, resulting from multiplying each of the four by the remaining three. Sensory experience and experiment witness the occurrence of all, and doubt has only occurred regarding the transformation of air into water. For it was said: The appearance of droplets on a vessel cooled by ice could be due to seepage (al-rashh) or due to the attraction of vapors (al-abkhira) to it, as Abu al-Barakat said: In the air surrounding the vessel there are fine watery particles, but due to their smallness and the attraction of the air's heat to them, they were unable to penetrate the air and descend onto the vessel. When their heat dissipated due to contact with the vessel cooled by ice, they condensed and became heavy, so they descended and gathered on the vessel. The first [view] is refuted because if it were due to seepage, hot water would be more likely for that since it is finer, and dew (al-nada) would only occur in places of seepage; moreover, seepage is only imagined in a vessel filled with ice, not in one that is covered. The second [view] is refuted because it is inconceivable that such an amount of watery particles would remain in hot summer air; rather, they must evaporate and rise. Even if it were conceded, then they should be exhausted or decrease with descent, so the droplets on the vessel would not return after being wiped away. And if it were claimed that they descended from a farther distance, then they would have taken a longer time, but the opposite is the case; moreover, descent occurs in a straight line, so how could it occur on the sides of the vessel?
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-defense argument concerning elemental transformations. It begins by classifying the possible transformations between the four classical elements (fire, air, water, earth) based on adjacency. Adjacent elements transform directly, giving three pairings. Non-adjacent elements transform via one or two intermediaries, yielding additional pairings. Each pairing involves two types of generation and corruption (one element changing into the other and vice versa). The total number of transformation types is calculated as twelve: six primary (direct), four with one intermediary, and two with two intermediaries—this is mathematically derived as 4 elements × 3 others. The author then asserts that all these transformations are empirically observed except the transformation of air into water, which has been doubted. To defend it, the author addresses a specific case: water droplets forming on a vessel cooled by ice. Two alternative explanations are presented: seepage (al-rashh) from inside the vessel, or attraction of vapors (al-abkhira) from the surrounding air as proposed by Abu al-Barakat. The author refutes the seepage explanation by noting that if it were seepage, hot water would be more likely to seep due to its fineness, and dew only appears where seepage occurs; moreover, seepage is only plausible in a vessel filled with ice, not one with ice placed on top (i.e., the outside). The vapor explanation is refuted because fine watery particles cannot persist in hot summer air; they would evaporate upward. Even if they did descend, they would be depleted, so droplets would not reappear after wiping. Also, descent in a straight line cannot account for droplets on the sides. Thus, the only remaining explanation is that air actually transforms into water, confirming that air-to-water transformation is real.

Key Terms
ازدواج
الكون والفساد
انقلاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Elemental transformations only occur between adjacent elements.
Correction: The passage shows that non-adjacent elements can transform via intermediaries, yielding additional pairings.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The twelve types of elemental transformation are systematically derived from the four elements and their adjacency relationships, and all except air-to-water are empirically confirmed.

Defending the Transformation of Air into Water
Defense of air-to-water transformation against alternative explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments defending the transformation of air into water against alternative physical explanations.

Core Idea
The appearance of water on a cooled vessel proves that air can transform into water.

Having enumerated the twelve types of elemental transformation, Sanusi now addresses the only one that has been doubted: the transformation of air into water. He presents two alternative explanations—seepage from inside the vessel and attraction of vapors—and refutes them both, thereby establishing that the only remaining explanation is genuine elemental transformation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt and alternative explanations
The doubt concerns water droplets forming on a vessel cooled by ice. Two alternative explanations are proposed: seepage from inside or condensation of vapors from the air.
These are based on Abu al-Barakat's theory.
2
Analyze the refutation of seepage
The author refutes seepage by noting that hot water would seep more, dew only appears where seepage occurs, and seepage is only plausible in a filled vessel, not one with ice placed on top.
This eliminates the first alternative.
3
Analyze the refutation of vapor attraction
The author argues that fine watery particles cannot persist in hot summer air; they would evaporate. Moreover, if they did descend, they would be depleted, and descent would be straight, not on the sides.
This eliminates the second alternative.
4
Conclude the defense
With both alternatives refuted, the only remaining explanation is that air actually transforms into water, confirming the empirical reality of this elemental change.

The Movement of the Celestial Sphere and the Sphere of Fire
Presents and refutes the argument that fire moves with the celestial sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that fire moves with the celestial sphere and its refutation based on the homogeneity of celestial matter.

Core Idea
Movement of an encompassing sphere does not force its contents to move, and homogeneity destroys natural attachment.

This passage tackles a classical cosmological question: does the sphere of fire move because the celestial sphere moves? The author first states a general principle, then presents an observational argument, a counterargument, and finally a refutation grounded in the nature of celestial and elemental bodies. Let's follow the lines of reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument unfolds from principle to evidence to counterargument to refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the opening principle
The author states that movement of the encompassing sphere does not necessitate movement of the encompassed when the center is united. This is a conceptual foundation.
This principle will be used later.
3
Analyze the evidence from meteors
The proponents use observable meteors and comets moving with the celestial sphere as evidence that fire moves with it. Also note why air does not follow.
The evidence is empirical.
4
Examine the counterargument and its refutation
A counterargument claims each part of fire has a specific natural place in the celestial sphere, so it follows. The refutation argues that because both the celestial sphere and fire are homogeneous, no part has a special attachment. That defeats the claim.
This is the key logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخفى أن حركة المحيط لا توجب حركة المحاط عند اتحاد المركز، لكن قد يتحرك بتبعيته لأسباب خارجية. وقد استدلوا بما يشاهد من حركات الشهب وذوات الأذناب على نهج حركة الفلك، أن كرة النار تتحرك بحركة الفلك، وأنما لم يتحرك الهواء تبعاً للنار، لأنه لرطوبته، وعدم بقاء أجزائه على أوضاعها ينفصل بسهولة. فلا يلزم جرم المحيط به. وقيل : إن كل جزء يفرض من النار له جزء معين من الفلك كالمكان الطبيعي له، وهو ملازم له ملاصق به طبعاً فيتبعه في الحركة. ورد بأن الفلك متشابه الأجزاء، وكذا النار الملاقية لكونها بسيطة، فيكون حال كل جزء من النار، مع كل جزء من الفلك كحاله مع سائر الأجزاء، فلا يكون البعض منها طالباً للبعض منه بالطبع.
It is hidden that the movement of the encompassing [sphere] does not necessitate the movement of the encompassed when the center is united, but it may move following it for external reasons. They have adduced as evidence what is observed of the motions of meteors and comets in the manner of the motion of the celestial sphere, that the sphere of fire moves with the motion of the celestial sphere, and that the air does not move following the fire because of its moisture and the non-endurance of its parts upon their positions, it separates easily. So the body of the encompassing sphere does not necessitate [the movement of the fire]. And it was said: Every part assumed from the fire has a specific part of the celestial sphere as its natural place, and it is inseparable from it, attached to it by nature, so it follows it in motion. It was refuted that the celestial sphere is homogeneous in its parts, and likewise the fire touching it because it is simple, so the state of every part of the fire with every part of the celestial sphere is like its state with the rest of the parts, so some of them are not naturally desiring of some of the others.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and its refutation regarding the motion of the sphere of fire. First, the author states a principle: the movement of an encompassing sphere does not necessarily move its contents if the center is shared, but it may move due to external causes. An argument from observation is then cited: meteors and comets move with the celestial sphere, indicating that the sphere of fire moves with it. Air does not follow because it is moist and its parts separate easily. Then a counterargument (قيل) is introduced: each part of fire has a specific part of the celestial sphere as its natural place, so it naturally clings and follows. The refutation (ورد) replies that the celestial sphere is homogeneous (متشابه الأجزاء) and the fire touching it is simple (بسيطة), so no part of fire has a specific attachment to any part of the sphere; therefore, the natural attachment claim fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the celestial sphere moves, everything inside it must move with it, including the sphere of fire, due to a natural attachment.
Correction: The refutation shows that because both the celestial sphere and fire are homogeneous, there is no specific natural attachment between parts. Thus, the celestial sphere's motion does not force fire to move by natural necessity.

Key Takeaway

The Rational Proofs for Sphericity, Centrality, and Stillness
The author states the main rational proofs for sphericity, centrality, and stillness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational rational proofs for the sphericity of the elements and the earth's centrality based on simplicity and absolute heaviness.

Core Idea
Simplicity proves sphericity; heaviness proves centrality.

The author begins by presenting the main proofs — the 'عمدة' — for the sphericity of all elements and the earth's position at the center. He argues that the very nature of these bodies, their simplicity and absolute heaviness, necessitates their spherical shape and central location. This rational approach contrasts with observational evidence, which will follow later.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage silently, then aloud if possible. Note the three claims: sphericity, centrality, stillness.
The translation is provided for help.
2
Identify the three proofs
Each claim has a one-word proof: simplicity, absolute heaviness, and absence of circular inclination.
These are rational, not empirical.
3
Understand 'simplicity'
Simplicity (بساطة) means uniformity; the simplest geometric shape is a sphere.
This is a key concept in medieval cosmology.
4
Connect heaviness to centrality
Absolute heaviness means earth must go to the lowest point, the center.
5
Reflect on stillness
The earth is still because it has only straight motion toward center, not circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والعمدة في كرية (الكل) بساطتها وفي كون الأرض في الوسط، ثقلها المطلق، وفي سكونها أنها في حيزها مع انتفاء الميل المستدير لوجود المستقيم، وقد يستدل على كرية الماء برؤية السائر في البحر أعالي الجبال قبل حضيضانها، وعلى توسط الأرض بعدم تفاوت الكواكب في الصغر والكبر بحسب البقاع، وعلى كريتها بكون طلوع الكواكب وغروبها، وارتفاع القطب وانخفاضه على نسبة واحدة بحسب البقاع وكأنه تحدث، وإلا فبعد تسليم انتفاء سبب آخر لا يفيد حقيقة الاستدارة والتوسط. = قد اتفق المحققون على أن العناصر كلها كرية الشكل، وأن الأرض في
He said: The main proof for the sphericity of all [elements] is their simplicity, and for the earth being in the center is its absolute heaviness, and for its stillness is that it is in its place with the absence of circular inclination due to the presence of straight inclination. And one may infer the sphericity of water from the observer at sea seeing the tops of mountains before their bases, and the centrality of the earth from the absence of variation in the size of stars according to regions, and its sphericity from the rising and setting of stars, and the elevation and lowering of the pole being in a fixed ratio according to regions, as if it were a [؟] . Otherwise, after conceding the absence of another cause, it does not yield the reality of roundness and centrality. = The investigators have agreed that all elements are spherical in shape, and that the earth is in [the center].
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the main proof (العمدة) for the sphericity of the celestial and elemental bodies and the earth's central position. The author states: For the sphericity of all, the proof is their simplicity (بساطتها). For the earth being in the center, the proof is its absolute heaviness (ثقل المطلق). For its stillness, the proof is that it is in its natural place, lacking circular inclination because straight inclination (downward motion) is present. Additional observational evidence follows: the sphericity of water is inferred from seeing mountain tops from a ship before their bases; the earth's centrality is inferred from the uniform size of stars across regions; and the earth's sphericity is inferred from the consistent ratios of star risings, settings, and pole elevation across regions. The author notes that if no other cause is admitted, these observations do not conclusively prove actual roundness and centrality. Finally, he reports the consensus of investigators that all elements are spherical and that the earth is at the center (the sentence is incomplete, presumably continuing 'في الوسط').

Correction: They are rational a priori proofs based on intrinsic properties.
