The Optical Mechanism of the Moon's Halo
Author details the reflection mechanism and why the cloud is invisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moon's halo is produced through reflection from tiny dew-like particles in a thin cloud.

Core Idea
Each dew droplet acts as a tiny mirror reflecting moonlight.

After defining the phenomena, the author provides a detailed causal explanation for the moon's halo. This lesson unpacks the physics: how polished dew-like parts in a thin cloud reflect the moon's light and why the cloud itself remains invisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal explanation
Read from 'يشير إلى سبب الهالة' to 'كأجزاء الهباء المتفرقة في الصحراء'. Focus on the role of the 'أجزاء رشية صقيلة'.
The author uses an analogy: mirrors and dust particles.
2
Identify the key components
List: thin delicate cloud, polished dew-like parts, reflection of light, and the condition for reflection (same direction).
Note that the cloud does not hide what is behind it.
3
Understand why the cloud is not seen
The author explains that the cloud is invisible because the moon's bright light overpowers the thin cloud, like dust particles unseen in sunlight.
This reinforces the optical nature of the phenomenon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد يحول) إليه بين القمر والبصر غيم رطب رقيق أبيض متصغر الأجزاء، تتفق أجزاء أوضاعها، فينعكس ضوء البصر من أجزاء ذلك الغيم إلى جرم القمر، فيؤدي كل جزء ضوء القمر، فيرى دائرة بيضاء تامة أو ناقصة، وتسمى الهالة. وقد تتضاعف وقد يحدث مثلها للشمس وتسمى الطغاوة، وإذا وقع مثل تلك الأجزاء في خلاف جهة الشمس حصل فيه من انعكاس شعاع البصر قوس، جانبها الأعلى أحمر ناصع، والأسفل أرجواني، والأوسط كرائي ويسمى قوس قزح. يشير إلى سبب الهالة وقوس قزح. أما الهالة فسببها إحاطة أجزاء رشية صقيلة كأنها مرايا متراصة بغيم رقيق لطيف لا يستر ما وراءه واقع في مقابلة القمر، فيرى في ذلك الغيم نفس القمر، لأن الشيء إنما يرى على الاستقامة نفسه لا شبحه، ويرى في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء الرشية شبحه لانعكاس ضوء البصر منها إلى القمر، لأن الضوء إذا وقع على صقيل انعكس إلى الجسم الذي وضعه من ذلك الصقيل كوضع المضيء منه إذا لم تكن جهته مخالفة لجهة المضيء، فيرى ضوء القمر شكله، لأن المرآة إذا كانت صغيرة لا تؤدي شكل المرئي، بل ضوءه ولا يرى لونه إن كان ملوناً، فيؤدي كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء ضوء القمر، فيرى دائرة مضيئة لكون الهيئة الحاصلة بين تلك الأجزاء، وبين المرئي واحدة، وإنما لا يرى السحاب الذي يقابل القمر لقوة شعاع القمر، فإن الرقيق اللطيف لا يرى في ضوء القوي كأجزاء الهباء المتفرقة في الصحراء، وأكثر ما تحدث الهالة عند عدم الريح، فيستدل بتخرقها من جميع الجهات على الصحو، ومن جهة على ريح تأتي من تلك الجهة، وببطلانها يثخن السحاب على المطر لتكثر الأجزاء المائية، وقد تتضاعف الهالة، بأن توجد سحابتان بالصفة المذكورة، إحداهما تحت الأخرى، ولا محالة تكون التحتانية أعظم لكونها أقرب. وذكر بعضهم أنه رأى هالات معاً، وأما سبع هالة الشمس وتسمى بالطغاوة فنادرة جداً.
He said (and it may change) between the moon and the sight a moist, thin, white cloud with small parts, their positions coinciding, then the light of sight reflects from the parts of that cloud to the body of the moon, so each part conveys the light of the moon, and a complete or incomplete white circle is seen, and it is called the halo. And it may be multiplied, and a similar thing may occur for the sun and is called the الطغاوة (sun halo). And if such parts occur opposite the direction of the sun, there occurs from the reflection of the ray of sight an arc, its upper side bright red, the lower violet, and the middle carmine, and it is called the rainbow. He points to the cause of the halo and the rainbow. As for the halo, its cause is the surrounding of polished dew-like parts, as if they are compact mirrors, by a thin, delicate cloud that does not conceal what is behind it, placed opposite the moon, then in that cloud the moon itself is seen, because a thing is seen directly itself, not its image, and in each one of those dew-like parts its image is seen because of the reflection of the light of sight from them to the moon, for when light falls on a polished surface, it reflects to the body that is positioned from that polished surface as the position of the luminous from it if its direction is not opposite the direction of the luminous, so the light of the moon and its shape are seen, because the mirror, if it is small, does not convey the shape of the seen object, but its light, and its color is not seen if it is colored, so each one of those parts conveys the light of the moon, and a luminous circle is seen because the configuration between those parts and the seen object is one. And the cloud that faces the moon is not seen because of the strength of the moon's ray, for the thin delicate is not seen in strong light, like the scattered dust particles in the desert. And most often the halo occurs when there is no wind, so it is inferred from its tearing from all directions to clear weather, and from one direction to wind coming from that direction, and by its invalidation, the cloud thickens to rain due to the abundance of watery parts. And the halo may be multiplied by the existence of two clouds with the described quality, one beneath the other, and inevitably the lower is larger because it is nearer. And some mentioned that they saw halos together. As for the sun's halo, called الطغاوة, it is very rare.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the halo (الهالة) and explains its physical cause. The author first describes the observable phenomenon: a white circle around the moon caused by a thin cloud with fine particles that reflect moonlight. He also mentions the sun's halo (الطغاوة) and gives a brief note on the rainbow (قوس قزح), which will be treated separately. The core explanation uses the principle of reflection: polished dew-like parts act like small mirrors; each reflects the moon's light to the eye, producing a luminous circle. The author clarifies that the cloud itself is not seen because the moon's light is stronger, similar to dust particles invisible in sunlight. Practical signs are also given: the halo's behavior can predict weather changes (clear skies or rain). The possibility of multiple halos is explained by stacked clouds, and the sun's halo is noted as very rare. The passage follows a pattern of definition, causal explanation, and application.

Key Terms
أجزاء رشية صقيلة
انعكاس
شعاع البصر
مرآة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moon's halo is a ring of cloud surrounding the moon.
Correction: The halo is actually a luminous circle caused by reflection from tiny particles in a thin cloud; the cloud itself is not seen.

Checkpoint
According to the explanation, why does each dew-like part only convey the light of the moon and not its shape?
A) Because the parts are too small to act as mirrors.
B) Because the mirror is small and cannot convey the shape of the seen object.
C) Because the moon's light is too strong.
D) Because the cloud is thick.

Key Takeaway
The moon's halo arises from countless tiny mirrored reflections from dew droplets, not from the cloud itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the moon's halo is not a ring of cloud but a grand illusion from countless tiny reflections. In your own spiritual life, where might you be mistaking a secondary cause (like a habit or person) for the true source of guidance? What reflection reveals the real cause?
?
The passage notes that the thin cloud is invisible because of the moon's strong light. What 'strong light' in your life—perhaps pride, busyness, or worldly success—makes you overlook the delicate signs of your Creator that are always present? How will you look more carefully today?
?
Just as a single dew droplet reflects only a fraction of moonlight yet contributes to a luminous circle, what small, seemingly insignificant act of worship or kindness could you perform today that might combine with others to create a lasting impact? What holds you back from offering that droplet?

Meteorological Indications of the Halo
Author gives weather predictions based on halo behavior and notes sun halo rarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the halo's occurrence and behavior can predict weather changes such as wind, clear skies, and rain.

Core Idea
The halo's patterns forecast wind direction and precipitation.

The author applies the halo to practical meteorology. He explains typical conditions for halo formation, how tearing patterns indicate wind, and how cloud thickening signals rain. This lesson also covers multiple halos and the rarity of the sun's halo.

Guided Path
1
Read the meteorological part
Read from 'وأكثر ما تحدث الهالة عند عدم الريح' to the end of the passage. Identify each sign mentioned.
Look for conditions: no wind, tearing directions, cloud thickening, multiple halos.
2
Map each sign to prediction
List: 1) Halo in calm weather → if tears from all sides → clear weather. 2) Tears from one side → wind from that side. 3) Halo disappears, cloud thickens → rain. 4) Multiple halos possible.
The author also notes the lower halo is larger due to proximity.
3
Note the sun's halo rarity
The passage concludes by stating the sun's halo (الطغاوة) is very rare. This contrasts with the more common moon halo.
This explains why practical signs are given mainly for the moon halo.

The Cause of the Rainbow (قوس قزح)
Author explains the cause of the rainbow using reflection from small water droplets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical cause of the rainbow as explained by classical Islamic scholars, including the necessary conditions, the reflection mechanism, and the geometric reasoning behind its arc shape.

Core Idea
The rainbow is an optical effect from sunlight reflecting off water droplets.

The author now turns from theological principles to a natural phenomenon: the rainbow. This passage offers a causal explanation grounded in optics and geometry, typical of the scientific tradition within Islamic scholarship. As you read, consider how the author combines empirical conditions (water droplets, sun position) with a geometric model to explain why the rainbow appears as an arc.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the causal structure: 'its cause is that...'
The text breaks off mid-explanation of colors.
2
Identify the conditions for the rainbow
List the conditions mentioned: watery parts opposite the sun, a dark background, the sun near the horizon. Ask why each condition is necessary.
The dark background (mountain/cloud) acts like a screen.
3
Understand the reflection mechanism
The passage says the ray of sight (or sun's ray) reflects from the polished water droplets. Each small droplet conveys only light, not the sun's shape.
This is a key point: the rainbow is not a direct image of the sun.
4
Examine the geometric explanation for the arc
The author uses a circle with the sun at the center. The visible arc is the part of the circle above the horizon. Consider why the arc gets smaller as the sun rises higher.
This explains why rainbows are not seen at noon.
5
Note the discussion of colors
The passage begins to explain colors by proximity to the sun, but it cuts off. Think about how this connects to later theories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوس قزح فسببه أنه إذا كان في خلاف جهة الشمس أجزاء مائية شفافة صافية، وكان وراءها جسم كثيف مثل جبل أو سحاب مظلم يكون كحال البلور الذي وراءه شيء ملون لينعكس منه الشعاع، وكانت الشمس قريبة من الأفق، فإذا واجهنا تلك الأجزاء المائية انعكس شعاع البصر من تلك الأجزاء الصقيلة إلى الشمس، فأدى كل واحد منها لكونه صغيراً ضوء الشمس دون شكلها وكان مستديراً على شكل قوس لأن الشمس لو جعلت بمركز دائرة لكان القدر الذي يقع من تلك الدائرة فوق الأرض يمر على تلك الأجزاء، ولو تمت الدائرة لكان تمامها تحت الأرض، وكلما كان ارتفاع الشمس أكثر، كان القوس أصغر، ولهذا لم يحدث إذا كانت الشمس في وسط السماء، وأما اختلاف ألوانها. فقيل لأن الناحية العليا تكون أقرب إلى الشمس فيكون انعكاس
As for the rainbow (قوس قزح), its cause is that if there are transparent, pure watery parts opposite the direction of the sun, and behind them a dense body such as a mountain or a dark cloud, which is like the case of crystal behind which is a colored thing so that the ray reflects from it, and the sun is near the horizon, then when we face those watery parts, the ray of sight reflects from those polished parts to the sun, so each one of them, because it is small, conveys the light of the sun without its shape, and it is curved in the shape of an arc because if the sun is placed at the center of a circle, the portion of that circle above the earth passes over those parts, and if the circle were completed, its entirety would be under the earth, and the higher the sun's altitude, the smaller the arc, and for this reason it does not occur when the sun is in the middle of the sky. As for the difference of its colors, it is said because the upper side is closer to the sun, so the reflection
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage provides the physical cause of the rainbow (قوس قزح). The author describes conditions: transparent water droplets opposite the sun, a dark background (like a mountain or cloud) to act as a screen, and the sun near the horizon. The mechanism involves reflection of the sight's ray (or sunlight) from the droplets to the sun. Each small droplet conveys only light, not the sun's shape, resulting in a curved arc. The arc's shape is explained geometrically: with the sun at the center of a circle, only the portion above the horizon is visible. The arc size decreases as the sun rises higher, which is why rainbows are not seen when the sun is overhead. The explanation of colors is cut off, but it begins to attribute them to the upper part being closer to the sun. The passage follows a causal structure: conditions, mechanism, geometric reasoning, and color difference.

Key Terms
قوس قزح
أجزاء مائية
انعكاس
شعاع البصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the rainbow not appear when the sun is at the zenith (mid-sky)?
A) The droplets evaporate due to heat.
B) The arc would be too large to see.
C) The geometric explanation shows the arc becomes very small or disappears.
D) The sun's light is too direct to cause reflection.

Key Takeaway
The rainbow is produced by reflection of sunlight from small water droplets, and its arc shape results from the geometry of the sun's position relative to the observer.

The Natural Causes of Lunar and Solar Halos
Explains the causes and characteristics of lunar and solar halos and their use in weather prediction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the optical mechanisms behind lunar and solar halos and how they serve as indicators of weather changes.

Core Idea
Halos are natural optical phenomena caused by water particles reflecting light.

In this passage from the coursebook, the author explains the causes of halos around the moon and sun, linking them to weather patterns. Instead of invoking supernatural causes, he provides a rational, empirical explanation based on the properties of light and water particles. This lesson unpacks that explanation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analysis. Notice the logical flow from the lunar halo to the solar halo.
The passage is dense with cause-effect relationships.
2
Identify the cause of the lunar halo
Focus on the first sentence: each water particle reflects moonlight, creating a luminous circle. Understand why the cloud opposite the moon is invisible.
3
Understand weather indications
The passage explains how the halo's behavior—tearing or thickening—predicts wind and rain. Note the role of water particles.
4
Note the doubling and solar halo
The halo can double when two cloud layers exist, and the solar halo (tughawa) is rare because the sun dissolves thin clouds.
This completes the explanation of all halo phenomena.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولونه إن كان ملوناً، فيؤدي كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء ضوء القمر، فيرى دائرة مضيئة لكون الهيئة الحاصلة بين تلك الأجزاء، وبين المرئي واحدة، وإنما لا يرى السحاب الذي يقابل القمر لقوة شعاع القمر، فإن الرقيق اللطيف لا يرى في ضوء القوي كأجزاء الهباء المتفرقة في الصحراء، وأكثر ما تحدث الهالة عند عدم الريح، فيستدل بتخرقها من جميع الجهات على الصحو، ومن جهة على ريح تأتي من تلك الجهة، وببطلانها يثخن السحاب على المطر لتكثر الأجزاء المائية، وقد تتضاعف الهالة، بأن توجد سحابتان بالصفة المذكورة، إحداهما تحت الأخرى، ولا محالة تكون التحتانية أعظم لكونها أقرب. وذكر بعضهم أنه رأى هالات معاً، وأما سبع هالة الشمس وتسمى بالطغاوة فنادرة جداً. لأن الشمس في الأكثر تحلل السحب الرقيقة.
And its color, if it is colored, then each of those parts conveys the moonlight, so a luminous circle is seen because the configuration between those parts and the seen object is one. And the cloud opposite the moon is not seen only because of the strength of the moon's ray, for the thin delicate is not seen in the light of the strong, like the scattered dust particles in the desert. And most often the halo occurs when there is no wind, so one infers from its tearing from all sides on clear [weather], and from a side on a wind coming from that side, and by its disappearance the clouds thicken for rain because the water particles increase. And the halo may be doubled, such that there are two clouds with the described quality, one below the other, and necessarily the lower is larger because it is closer. And some mentioned that they saw halos together. As for the seven[?] halo of the sun, called al-tughawa, it is very rare, because the sun mostly dissolves thin clouds.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage explains the natural causes of the lunar halo (hala) and the rare solar halo (tughawa). It begins with the lunar halo: if the halo is colored, each water particle reflects moonlight, creating a bright circle. The cloud opposite the moon is invisible due to the moon's strong light, similar to how fine dust is unseen in strong sunlight. Halos usually occur in calm weather, so their breaking indicates fair weather, while their persistence and thickening clouds predict rain. The halo can double when two cloud layers exist, the lower larger. Some have observed multiple halos. The solar halo, called 'tughawa,' is rare because the sun's heat dissolves thin clouds.

Key Terms
هالة (hala)
طغاوة (tughawa)
شعاع (shu'a')
أجزاء مائية (ajza' ma'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Halos are supernatural omens or divine signs unrelated to natural causes.
Correction: The passage explains halos as natural optical phenomena caused by water particles reflecting light, and their behavior correlates with weather changes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary cause of the lunar halo?
A) Reflection of moonlight from water particles
B) Diffraction of sunlight
C) The moon's own light
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Lunar and solar halos are natural phenomena caused by water particles reflecting light and can be used to predict weather.

Cause and Shape of the Rainbow
Sanusi explains the cause and shape of the rainbow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical cause of the rainbow's arc and how its size depends on the sun's elevation.

Core Idea
The rainbow is an arc of reflected sunlight from water particles.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explains the natural phenomenon of the rainbow. It sets out the necessary conditions—water particles opposite the sun, a dark background, and the sun near the horizon—and describes how the arc is formed. The size of the arc varies with the sun's height, disappearing at noon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the precise conditions listed.
2
Identify the conditions for rainbow formation
List the three conditions mentioned: water particles opposite the sun, a dark background, and the sun near the horizon.
3
Understand the geometric explanation of the arc
The text explains the arc using a circle with the sun as center. Consider why only a part of the circle is visible above the earth.
This explains why the rainbow is a part of a circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوس قزح فسببه أنه إذا كان في خلاف جهة الشمس أجزاء مائية شفافة صافية، وكان وراءها جسم كثيف مثل جبل أو سحاب مظلم يكون كحال البلور الذي وراءه شيء ملون لينعكس منه الشعاع، وكانت الشمس قريبة من الأفق، فإذا واجهنا تلك الأجزاء المائية انعكس شعاع البصر من تلك الأجزاء الصقيلة إلى الشمس، فأدى كل واحد منها لكونه صغيراً ضوء الشمس دون شكلها وكان مستديراً على شكل قوس لأن الشمس لو جعلت بمركز دائرة لكان القدر الذي يقع من تلك الدائرة فوق الأرض يمر على تلك الأجزاء، ولو تمت الدائرة لكان تمامها تحت الأرض، وكلما كان ارتفاع الشمس أكثر، كان القوس أصغر، ولهذا لم يحدث إذا كانت الشمس في وسط السماء، وأما اختلاف ألوانها. فقيل لأن الناحية العليا تكون أقرب إلى الشمس فيكون انعكاس الضوء أقوى، فيرى حمرة ناصعة، والسفلى أبعد منها، وأقل إشراقاً، فترى حمرة في سواد وهو الأرجواني، ويتولد بينهما كرائي مركب من إشراق الحمرة وكدر الظلمة. ورد بأن ذلك يقتضي أن يتدرج من نصوع الحمرة إلى الأرجوانية من غير انفصال الألوان بعضها عن بعض، على أن تولد الكرائي إنما هو من الأصفر والأسود، فليس له مع الأحمر والأرجواني كثير مناسبة. واعترف ابن سينا بعدم الاطلاع على سبب اختلاف هذه الألوان.
As for the rainbow, its cause is that when opposite the direction of the sun there are transparent, clear water particles, and behind them a dense body such as a mountain or dark cloud, like the case of crystal behind which there is a colored object so that the ray reflects from it, and the sun is near the horizon. When we face those water particles, the ray of sight reflects from those polished particles to the sun, so each of them, because it is small, conveys the light of the sun without its form, and it is curved in the shape of an arc, because if the sun were made the center of a circle, the part of that circle above the earth would pass over those particles, and if the circle were completed, its completion would be under the earth. And the higher the sun's elevation, the smaller the arc, and therefore it does not occur when the sun is at mid-sky. As for the variation of its colors, it is said because the upper side is closer to the sun, so the reflection of light is stronger, so a pure red is seen; the lower is farther from it and less bright, so a red in blackness is seen, which is purple; and between them a yellowish-green is generated composed of the brightness of red and the turbidity of darkness. It was objected that this requires a gradation from pure red to purple without separation of colors from one another, while the generation of yellowish-green is only from yellow and black, so it has no much suitability with red and purple. And Ibn Sina acknowledged not knowing the cause of the variation of these colors.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the rainbow (qaws quzah). It starts with the conditions: water particles opposite the sun, a dark background (e.g., mountain or cloud), and the sun near the horizon. The ray of sight reflects from the particles to the sun, and each particle conveys the sun's light without its shape, forming an arc. The arc's size depends on the sun's elevation: higher sun means smaller arc; at zenith no rainbow. The colors are explained by distance: upper part closer to sun gives strong red, lower gives purple, and the middle yellowish-green from mixing. An objection follows: this explanation would produce a continuous gradation, but actual rainbow colors are distinct. Also, yellowish-green supposedly comes from yellow and black, not red and purple. Finally, Ibn Sina admits ignorance of the true cause of color variation.

Key Terms
قوس قزح (qaws quzah)
انعكاس (in'ikas)
شعاع البصر (shu'a' al-basar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the rainbow's size when the sun is higher in the sky?
A) Gets larger
B) Gets smaller
C) Stays the same
D) Disappears completely

Key Takeaway
The rainbow is an optical phenomenon caused by reflection of sunlight from water particles, and its arc size depends on the sun's altitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a rainbow, do you immediately think of the Creator's precise design or do you dismiss it as a mere natural occurrence? How will you intentionally cultivate gratitude and awe next time you witness this phenomenon?
?
This passage reveals that the rainbow requires specific conditions: water particles, a dark background, and the sun at a low angle. Does knowing these details make you more or less amazed at Allah's wisdom? Honestly, how does your heart respond to such intricate explanations?

Color Variation and Objection
Sanusi discusses the colors of the rainbow, an objection, and Ibn Sina's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will analyze the proposed explanation of rainbow colors and the objection that highlights its inconsistency.

Core Idea
The rainbow's colors are not continuous, contradicting the explanation.

This passage continues the discussion of the rainbow, focusing on its colors. An explanation is given based on the distance from the sun, but an objection points out that this would produce gradual shading, not distinct bands. The passage ends with Ibn Sina's admission of ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of color formation
Read the part describing how colors are formed according to the distance from the sun. Note the three colors mentioned: pure red, purple, and yellowish-green.
Pay attention to the terms used.
2
Understand the objection
The objection argues that this explanation would lead to a continuous gradient, not distinct bands. Also, the mixture theory for yellowish-green is inconsistent.
This is a critique of the proposed causal mechanism.
3
Note Ibn Sina's acknowledgment
Ibn Sina admits he does not know the cause of the color variation. This shows the humility of a scholar.
This is a famous admission from a great philosopher.

The Rainbow and Its Colors: Objections and Observations
Author explains rainbow colors, refutes an objection, and reports a lunar rainbow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the medieval Islamic explanation of rainbow colors, the objection to that explanation, Ibn Sina's admission of ignorance, and a reported observation of a lunar rainbow.

Core Idea
Rainbow colors defied easy explanation, even for Ibn Sina; lunar rainbows are exceptionally rare.

In this passage, the author of Sharh al-Maqasid discusses the generation of rainbow colors, presents an objection to the standard explanation, and then cites Ibn Sina's confession of ignorance. He also shares his own observation of a lunar rainbow, a phenomenon he notes is rarely discussed. This lesson will walk you through the key points of this natural-philosophical discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage once, then follow with the literal English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
The passage contains both theoretical reasoning and a personal observation.
2
Examine the color explanation and the objection
Notice how the author describes the colors: brilliant red from stronger light, violet from weaker light, and an intermediate color from mixture. Then identify the objection that demands a continuous gradation.
The objection questions whether the colors can be distinct if they arise from a gradient of light intensity.
3
Reflect on Ibn Sina's admission and the lunar rainbow
Consider what Ibn Sina's admission reveals about the state of knowledge. Then compare the description of the lunar rainbow with that of the solar rainbow.
The lunar rainbow was observed in Turkestan in 763 AH.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضوء أقوى، فيرى حمرة ناصعة، والسفلى أبعد منها، وأقل إشراقاً، فترى حمرة في سواد وهو الأرجواني، ويتولد بينهما كرائي مركب من إشراق الحمرة وكدر الظلمة . ورد بأن ذلك يقتضي أن يتدرج من نصوع الحمرة إلى الأرجوانية من غير انفصال الألوان بعضها عن بعض، على أن تولد الكرائي إنما هو من الأصفر والأسود، فليس له مع الأحمر والأرجواني كثير مناسبة . واعترف ابن سينا بعدم الاطلاع على سبب اختلاف هذه الألوان . قال وقد شاهدت مثل ذلك للقمر في ليلة رشية الجو، إلا أنه كان أصغر كثيف الألوان قريباً من تمام دائرة . ذكروا أن القمر قد يحدث على الندرة قوساً خيالية، لا يكون لها ألوان، لكني قد شاهدت بتركستان في سنة ثلاث وستين وسبعمائة في خلاف جهة القمر قوساً على ألوان قوس قزح إلا أنها كانت أصغر منها كثيراً ، وكانت بحيث تكاد تتم دائرة ، ولم تكن ألوانها في ضياء صفاء الألوان الشمسية وإشراقها بل أكثف، وكان ذلك في ليلة رشية الجو رقيقة السحاب، والقمر على قرب من الأفق.
The light is stronger, so a brilliant red is seen, and the lower (ray) is farther from it and less bright, so a red in blackness is seen, which is violet, and between them arises a color composed of the brightness of red and the murkiness of darkness. It was objected that this would require a gradation from pure red to violet without separation of the colors from one another, given that the generation of the color is only from yellow and black, so it has little connection with red and violet. And Ibn Sina acknowledged not being informed of the cause of the difference of these colors. He said: And I observed something like that for the moon on a moist night, except that it was smaller, with dense colors, nearly a complete circle. They mentioned that the moon may rarely produce an imaginary bow without colors, but I observed in Turkestan in the year 763 in the opposite direction of the moon a bow with the colors of the rainbow except that it was much smaller, and it was such that it nearly formed a complete circle, and its colors were not in the brightness and clarity of solar colors and their brilliance, but denser, and that was on a moist night with thin clouds, and the moon near the horizon.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a natural-philosophical discussion of the rainbow and similar phenomena. The author begins by explaining that when light is stronger, a brilliant red is seen, and the lower part of the bow is farther and less bright, producing a red mixed with blackness (violet). Between them arises a color compounded of brightness and murkiness. He then presents an objection: this explanation would require a continuous gradation from pure red to violet without distinct color bands, and moreover the generated color (impure red) supposedly comes from yellow and black, not from red and violet. The author does not assert the objection but reports it. He then notes that Ibn Sina confessed ignorance about the cause of the different colors. Next, the author reports his own observation of a lunar rainbow (on a moist night) that was smaller with dense colors, nearly forming a full circle. He contrasts this with the common claim that the moon rarely produces a colorless bow, and states that he personally saw a bow with rainbow colors in Turkestan in 763 AH. That lunar bow was much smaller, nearly circular, and its colors were denser and less brilliant than the solar rainbow. This occurred on a moist night with thin clouds, when the moon was near the horizon. The passage thus combines a theoretical explanation, an objection, an authoritative admission of ignorance, and an empirical report.

Key Terms
قوس قزح
أرجواني
كرائي
ابن سينا
قمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) He denied the existence of the rainbow.
D) He claimed the colors are an illusion.

Key Takeaway
The rainbow's colors were a challenge to medieval natural philosophers, and even Ibn Sina admitted ignorance; rare lunar rainbows were also observed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author observed a lunar rainbow and reported it despite it being rare. Are you willing to trust your own observations and experiences even when they contradict what others say, as long as they align with truth? What observation have you dismissed because it seemed rare or unlikely?

The Cause of Stone Formation
Enumeration of causes of stone formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary cause of petrification according to the author, including the role of heat and viscous clay, and how water can also turn to stone.

Core Idea
Heat acting on viscous clay is the main cause of stones becoming hard.

In this passage, the author explains the second type of things that occur on earth: stones and mountains. He focuses first on the formation of stones, identifying the most common cause as the action of heat on viscous clay. He also discusses how even liquid water can solidify into stone under certain conditions. This lesson examines those causal explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's wording set the stage.
2
Identify the primary cause of petrification
Look for the phrase 'السبب الأكثر لتحجر الأرض' and note what it is: the action of heat on viscous clay.
The author uses 'عمل الحرارة في الطين اللزج'.
3
Explore the secondary cause
Notice that the author also says liquid water can turn into stone. Find the two sub-causes: mineral petrifying power or earthy dominance.
The example of salt is given.
4
Understand the conditions for a great stone
The author concludes with the condition for forming a great stone: great heat meeting abundant viscous clay, either instantly or over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال النوع الثاني ما يحدث على الأرض مثل الأحجار والجبال والسبب الأكثر لتحجر الأرض، عمل الحرارة في الطين اللزج، بحيث يستحكم انعقاد رطبه بيابسه، وقد ينعقد الماء السيال حجراً، إما لقوة معدنية محجرة أو لأرضية غالبة على ذلك الماء بالقوة لا بالمقدار كما في الملح، فإذا صادف الحر العظيم طيناً كثيراً لزجاً إما دفعة وإما على مرور الأيام يتكون الحجر العظيم، فإذا ارتفع بأن تجعل الزلزلة العظيمة طائفة من الأرض تلا التلال، ثم تحجر تحجر أو بأن يكون الطين المتحجر مختلف الأجزاء في الصلابة والرخاوة، فتحفر أجزاؤه الرخوة بالمياه والرياح، وتغور تلك الحفر بالتدريج غوراً شديداً، وتبقى الصلبة مرتفعة أو بغير ذلك من الأسباب فهو الجبل، أو يحصل من تراكم عمارات تخربت وقد يرى بعض الجبال منضودة سافا فسافا كأنها سافات الجدار، فيشبه أن يكون حدوث مادة الفوقاني بعد تحجر التحتاني، وقد سال على كل ساف من خلاف جوهره ما صار حائلاً بينه وبين الآخر، وقد يوجد في كثير من الأحجار عند كسرها أجزاء الحيوانات المائية، فيشبه أن هذه المعمورات قد كانت في سالف الدهر مغمورة في البحر، فحصل الطين اللزج الكثير، وتحجر بعد الانكشاف. فلذلك كثر الجبال، ويكون الحفر ما بينها بأسباب تقتضيه كالسيول والرياح ومن منافع الجبال حفظ الأبخرة التي هي مادة المعادن
He said: The second type: what occurs on the earth, such as stones and mountains. And the most common cause for the petrification of earth is the action of heat on viscous clay, such that the binding of its moisture with its dry parts becomes strong. And liquid water may turn into stone, either due to a mineral petrifying power or due to an earthy property dominating that water in power not in quantity, as in salt. So when great heat meets abundant viscous clay, either at once or over the passage of days, a great stone is formed. Then if it rises—because a great earthquake makes a portion of the earth like hills, then it petrifies, or because the petrified clay has parts differing in hardness and softness, so its soft parts are excavated by water and winds, and those excavations sink deeply gradually, and the hard parts remain elevated—or by other causes, then that is a mountain. Or it arises from the accumulation of ruined buildings. And one may see some mountains layered layer upon layer as if they were layers of a wall, and it seems that the formation of the upper material occurred after the petrification of the lower, and on each layer flowed from its substance something that became a barrier between it and the other. And in many stones when broken there are found parts of aquatic animals, so it seems that these inhabited lands were in ancient times submerged in the sea, then abundant viscous clay occurred, and it petrified after the uncovering. Therefore mountains are abundant, and the hollows between them arise from causes that necessitate them, such as torrents and winds. And among the benefits of mountains is the preservation of vapors which are the material of minerals.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of terrestrial formations. The author introduces the second type of phenomena: things that occur on earth, namely stones and mountains. He identifies the primary cause of petrification (tahajjur) as the action of heat on viscous clay (ṭīn lazij), such that the moisture tightly binds with the dry parts. He notes that even liquid water may solidify into stone, either due to a mineral petrifying power or because an earthy quality dominates the water in potency (not in quantity), as seen in salt. Then he explains the formation of a great stone when intense heat meets abundant viscous clay, either instantaneously or gradually. For the formation of a mountain, he gives several mechanisms: (1) an earthquake elevates a portion of earth into hills, and then it petrifies; (2) the petrified clay has parts of varying hardness, so soft parts are eroded by water and wind, leaving hard parts elevated—this is a mountain; (3) accumulation of ruined buildings. He also notes that some mountains appear stratified, suggesting successive layers formed one after another, each separated by a different substance. He cites evidence from fossils: in many stones when broken, parts of aquatic animals are found, implying that these lands were once submerged in the sea, then clay accumulated and petrified after the water receded. This explains the abundance of mountains. The hollows between mountains are caused by factors like torrents and winds. Finally, he mentions a benefit of mountains: they preserve vapors that are the material for minerals. The passage thus combines empirical observation (fossils, stratification) with natural philosophical causes (heat, erosion, earth movements).

Key Terms
تحجر
طين لزج
حرارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Water cannot turn into stone.
Correction: The author explains that water can turn into stone under certain conditions, such as a mineral petrifying power or earthy dominance.

Checkpoint
What is the most common cause of petrification according to the passage?
A) The action of cold on clay
B) The action of heat on viscous clay
C) The pressure of winds
D) The accumulation of ruins

Key Takeaway
The most common cause of stone formation is the action of heat on viscous clay, which causes moisture and dry parts to bind firmly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a stone or mountain, do you pause to reflect on the precise causes Allah has put in place for its formation? If not, what is one way you can remind yourself of His wisdom when you encounter nature today?
?
The text describes that great stones can form either instantly or over many days. Think of a trial or blessing in your life that came suddenly versus gradually. Did you recognize Allah's hand in both? What keeps you from seeing His action in slow processes?
?
The author distinguishes between primary (heat + clay) and secondary (water to stone) causes. Are you so focused on the immediate physical causes of your circumstances that you forget the ultimate Cause, Allah? What is one habit you can change to shift your trust from means to the Creator of means?

The Formation of Mountains and Their Evidence
Explanation of mountain formation mechanisms and supporting evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mechanisms of mountain formation as described by the author, the evidence from stratified layers and fossils, and the benefits of mountains.

Core Idea
Mountains form from earth movements, erosion, or accumulation; fossils show they were once underwater.

After explaining stone formation, the author turns to mountains. He describes multiple ways mountains can arise: earthquakes lifting the earth, differential erosion of hard and soft rocks, or accumulation of ruins. He also points to observable evidence—stratified layers and fossils—to support the theory that many mountains were once seabeds. Finally, he notes the benefit of mountains in preserving mineral vapors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the transition from stone formation to mountain formation.
2
List the mechanisms of mountain formation
Identify three mechanisms: earthquake uplift, differential erosion, and accumulation of ruins. Find the Arabic phrases for each.
Look for 'الزلزلة العظيمة', 'مختلف الأجزاء في الصلابة والرخاوة', and 'تراكم عمارات تخربت'.
3
Examine the evidence
Find the two pieces of evidence: stratified layers ('منضودة سافا فسافا') and fossils of aquatic animals ('أجزاء الحيوانات المائية').
The author uses these to argue that lands were once submerged.
4
Understand the benefit of mountains
The last sentence gives the benefit: preserving vapors that are the material for minerals.

The Formation of Stones and Mountains: Natural Causes in Creation
Explains the second type of earthly phenomena: stones and mountains formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic scholars explained the natural processes behind the formation of stones and mountains, integrating observation with theological reflection.

Core Idea
Stones and mountains form through natural causes like heat and erosion, revealing wisdom in creation.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a detailed naturalistic account of how stones and mountains come to be. It shows that the author, while affirming divine creation, does not ignore secondary causality. The explanation combines empirical observation (fossils, layering) with reasoning about heat, clay, and water. By studying this, we see how kalam thinkers engaged with the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main types
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. The author introduces the second type of earthly phenomena: stones and mountains. Notice that he starts with a general cause before delving into specifics.
The passage is long; take your time to absorb the flow.
2
Understand the primary cause of petrification
Focus on the statement that the greater cause for petrification is heat acting on viscous clay. The author also mentions that flowing water can turn to stone under certain conditions.
Compare this with modern geology's understanding of sedimentary rock formation.
3
Examine the two processes for mountain formation
The passage describes two main ways mountains form: (1) through earthquakes that lift earth which then petrifies, and (2) through differential erosion where soft parts are removed, leaving hard parts elevated. There is also mention of accumulation of ruins.
Note that the author considers multiple pathways, showing flexibility.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why the author includes details like fossilized aquatic animals and layering. These observations point to a deliberate design and historical changes in the earth's surface, affirming the wisdom of the Creator.
This connects to the broader kalam theme of using nature as a sign of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال النوع الثاني ما يحدث على الأرض مثل الأحجار والجبال والسبب قد يصادف الحر العظيم طبعاً كثير الرخاء، فينعقد حجراً وتحفر الرياح والمياه أجزاءه الرخوة فتبقى الصلبة مرتفعة هي الجبال، وقد يتكون من تراكم عمارات تخربت ومن غير ذلك، ثم لصلابتها تبقى فيها الأبخرة، ولقلة تسخنها بانعكاس الشعاع تبقى عليها الثلوج والأنداء، فتكون المعادن والسحب والعيون. مثل الأحجار والجبال والسبب الأكثر لتحجر الأرض، عمل الحرارة في الطين اللزج، بحيث يستحكم انعقاد رطبه بيابسه، وقد ينعقد الماء السيال حجراً، إما لقوة معدنية محجرة أو لأرضية غالبة على ذلك الماء بالقوة لا بالمقدار كما في الملح، فإذا صادف الحر العظيم طيناً كثيراً لزجاً إما دفعة وإما على مرور الأيام يتكون الحجر العظيم، فإذا ارتفع بأن تجعل الزلزلة العظيمة طائفة من الأرض تلا التلال، ثم تحجر تحجر أو بأن يكون الطين المتحجر مختلف الأجزاء في الصلابة والرخاوة، فتحفر أجزاؤه الرخوة بالمياه والرياح، وتغور تلك الحفر بالتدريج غوراً شديداً، وتبقى الصلبة مرتفعة أو بغير ذلك من الأسباب فهو الجبل، أو يحصل من تراكم عمارات تخربت وقد يرى بعض الجبال منضودة سافا فسافا كأنها سافات الجدار، فيشبه أن يكون حدوث مادة الفوقاني بعد تحجر التحتاني، وقد سال على كل ساف من خلاف جوهره ما صار حائلاً بينه وبين الآخر، وقد يوجد في كثير من الأحجار عند كسرها أجزاء الحيوانات المائية، فيشبه أن هذه المعمورات قد كانت في سالف الدهر مغمورة في البحر، فحصل الطين اللزج الكثير، وتحجر بعد الانكشاف. فلذلك كثر الجبال، ويكون الحفر ما بينها بأسباب تقتضيه كالسيول والرياح ومن منافع الجبال حفظ الأبخرة التي هي مادة المعادن
He said: The second type: what happens on the earth, such as stones and mountains. The cause: great heat may encounter very soft earth, so it becomes stone, and winds and waters dig its soft parts, leaving the hard parts elevated – they are the mountains. It may also form from the accumulation of ruined buildings and other things. Then, due to their hardness, vapors remain in them, and because they are little heated by the reflection of rays, snow and dews remain on them, thus producing minerals, clouds, and springs. Such as stones and mountains. The greater cause for the petrification of the earth is the action of heat on viscous clay, such that the binding of its moisture with its dryness becomes firm. Flowing water may also become stone, either due to a petrifying mineral power or to an earthiness overwhelming that water in power not in quantity, as in salt. When great heat meets much viscous clay, either at once or over the course of days, great stone forms. If it rises – either by a great earthquake making a portion of earth into hills, then it petrifies, or by the petrified clay having parts differing in hardness and softness, so its soft parts are dug by waters and winds, and those hollows gradually sink deeply, while the hard parts remain elevated – or by other causes, that is the mountain. Or it results from the accumulation of ruined buildings. Some mountains are seen layered course upon course, like the courses of a wall, suggesting that the upper material formed after the lower petrified, and upon each course flowed something from a different substance that became a barrier between it and the next. In many stones when broken, parts of aquatic animals are found, suggesting that these inhabited lands were in ancient times submerged in the sea, so much viscous clay obtained and petrified after exposure. Hence mountains are many. The hollows between them are formed by causes that require them, such as torrents and winds. Among the benefits of mountains is the preservation of vapors that are the material of minerals.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the second type of earthly phenomena: the formation of stones and mountains. The author begins by stating the type and its examples: stones and mountains. He then explains the primary cause: intense heat acting on soft earth, causing it to harden into stone, while softer parts are eroded by wind and water, leaving the hard parts elevated as mountains. He also mentions alternative origins, such as accumulation of ruined buildings. The passage describes how vapors are trapped in mountains due to their hardness, and how snow and dew remain on them due to minimal heating from reflected sunlight, ultimately producing minerals, clouds, and springs. The author then elaborates on the more common cause of petrification: heat acting on viscous clay, leading to firm concretion. He notes that even flowing water can turn to stone, either by a petrifying mineral force or by an earthy quality overcoming the water's inherent power, analogously to salt formation. When intense heat meets abundant viscous clay, large stones form gradually or instantaneously. The elevation of these stones into mountains occurs either by earthquakes lifting portions of earth that then petrify, or by differential hardness within the clay: softer parts erode, leaving harder parts as mountains. Alternatively, mountains may form from accumulated ruins. The author then observes that some mountains show distinct layered structures, suggesting successive formation of upper layers after lower ones solidified, with different materials between layers. He further notes that many stones contain fossilized aquatic animal parts, indicating that the land was once submerged, clay accumulated, and after the sea retreated, the clay petrified. Thus, mountains are abundant, and the valleys between them are formed by water and wind erosion. Finally, he mentions one benefit of mountains: they trap vapors, which later become the material for minerals. The passage presents a naturalistic, causal explanation of geological formation, consistent with the kalam interest in highlighting wisdom and order in creation.

الجبال
الطين اللزج
التحجر
الزلزلة
الأبخرة
المعادن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mountains are created instantaneously without natural processes.
Correction: The passage shows gradual formation through heat, pressure, and erosion over time, while still recognizing divine causation.

Checkpoint

A) The accumulation of ruined buildings
B) The action of heat on viscous clay
C) The evaporation of water
D) The impact of earthquakes

Key Takeaway
Stones and mountains are not arbitrary but result from observable natural processes that manifest divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author describes how mountains form through gradual processes like erosion and heat. Reflect on a 'mountain' in your own life—a persistent difficulty or challenge. Can you see it as part of Allah's wisdom, being shaped over time? Will you trust the process rather than demand instant relief?
?
You find fossils in mountains, evidence of land once submerged. What does that say about the impermanence of this world? Are you living as if this world will last forever, or are you preparing for the ultimate change?
?
The passage attributes the formation of stones to the action of heat on clay. In your spiritual life, what 'heat' (trials, difficult situations) is Allah using to solidify your faith? Are you resisting or allowing yourself to be shaped?

The Natural Cause of Earthquakes
Explains the cause and nature of earthquakes as trapped vapors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the natural mechanism of earthquakes as explained in classical Islamic natural philosophy, particularly the role of trapped vapors and dense earth crust.

Core Idea
Earthquakes are caused by trapped vapors struggling to escape from beneath dense earth.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a natural explanation for earthquakes, attributing them to physical processes rather than purely supernatural causes. The author describes how vapor or smoke trapped under a dense crust tries to escape, causing the ground to shake and sometimes split. Understanding this perspective helps us appreciate how pre-modern scholars integrated empirical observation with theological frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own causal sequence come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the key elements
Notice the trapped substance (vapor/smoke/wind), the dense crust of the earth, the struggle to exit, and the resulting effects: shaking, splitting, fire, and sounds.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this natural explanation coexists with the author's theological context. Note the exception of soft lands, which confirms the causal model.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال النوع الثالث ما يحدث في الأرض قد يحتبس فيها بخار أو دخان أو نحو ذلك، ووجه الأرض متكاثف فيتحرك، ويحرك الأرض، وربما يشقها، فتحدث الزلازل، وقد يكون معها نار محرقة وأصوات هائلة. ويسمى قد يعرض لجزء من الأرض حركة بسبب ما يتحرك تحتها، فيحرك ما فوقه الزلزلة، وذلك إذا تولد تحت الأرض بخار أو دخان أو ريح أو ما يناسب ذلك، وكان وجه الأرض متكاثف عديم المسام أو ضيقها جداً. وحاول ذلك الخروج، ولم يتمكن لكثافة الأرض، تحرك في ذاته وحرك الأرض، وربما يشقها لقوته، وقد ينفصل منه نار محرقة، وأصوات هائلة لشدة المحاكة والمصاكة، وقد منها دوي لشدة الريح، ولا توجد الزلزلة في الأراضي الرخوة لسهولة خروج يسمع بهم في موج كالجبال.
He said: The third type: what happens in the earth. Vapor or smoke or something similar may be trapped in it, while the face of the earth is dense, so it moves and shakes the earth, and may split it, thus earthquakes occur, and there may be with it burning fire and tremendous sounds. It is called: a portion of the earth may undergo a movement due to something moving beneath it, so that movement shakes what is above it – that is the earthquake. This occurs when vapor, smoke, wind, or something similar is generated beneath the earth, and the face of the earth is dense, lacking pores or having very narrow ones. That thing tries to exit but cannot due to the density of the earth, so it moves in itself and shakes the earth, and may split it by its force. Burning fire and tremendous sounds may separate from it due to severe friction and collision, and there may be a roar due to the force of the wind. Earthquakes do not occur in soft lands because of the ease of exit – [you hear a sound] like waves like mountains.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of earthly phenomena: earthquakes. The author begins by stating the type and immediately describes the mechanism: vapor, smoke, or similar substances become trapped beneath the earth's surface, while the earth's crust is dense. The trapped matter moves, causing the earth to shake and sometimes crack, resulting in earthquakes, often accompanied by fire and loud sounds. The author then provides a more detailed explanation: a portion of the earth moves because something beneath it moves, and that movement is called 'the earthquake' (الزلزلة). This happens when vapor, smoke, wind, or similar is generated underground, and the earth's crust is dense with very few or no pores. The trapped substance tries to escape but cannot due to density; so it moves internally, shaking the earth, and may even split it with force. During this process, burning fire and tremendous sounds separate from the substance due to severe friction and collision, and a roar is heard from the force of the wind. The author concludes that earthquakes do not occur in soft lands because the trapped substances can easily escape. The passage presents a natural causal explanation for earthquakes, attributing them to physical processes rather than purely supernatural causes.

الزلزلة
بخار
دخان

نار محرقة
أصوات هائلة

Correction: The passage explains earthquakes as natural phenomena caused by trapped vapors under dense ground, though this does not deny a divine role in the causal chain.

Earthquakes are natural events caused by physical processes, specifically trapped vapors under a dense earth crust, as explained in classical Islamic philosophy.

The Natural Cause of Earthquakes
Defines earthquakes and explains their physical cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical explanation of earthquakes as presented in classical Islamic cosmology, including the roles of trapped vapors and earth density.

Core Idea
Earthquakes have natural causes: trapped vapors in compact earth.

In this passage, the author explains earthquakes as a natural phenomenon caused by the confinement of vapors or smoke beneath a compact earth surface. This scientific perspective, rooted in Aristotelian physics, shows that Islamic scholars did not see earthquakes as purely supernatural but as part of God's natural order. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses technical terms like 'بخار' and 'تكاثف'.
2
Identify the core cause
Find where the author states the cause of earthquakes. Look for words like 'يحتبس' (trapped) and 'بخار' (vapor).
3
Understand the conditions
Note the condition that the earth's surface must be compact and without pores. Why is this necessary?
The author contrasts this with soft lands where earthquakes do not occur.
4
List the effects
What happens when the trapped vapor tries to escape? Identify the sequence: movement, shaking, splitting, fire, sounds.
These effects are described in a chain of cause and effect.
5
Consider practical and external factors
Note the author's observations about wells reducing earthquakes and solar eclipses causing them. How do these fit into the natural explanation?
This shows the author integrates empirical observations and astronomical events.

He said: The third type is what occurs in the earth. Vapor or smoke or something similar may be confined in it, and the face of the earth is compact, so it moves and shakes the earth, and perhaps splits it, causing earthquakes. It may be accompanied by burning fire and tremendous sounds. It is called... A part of the earth may experience movement due to what moves beneath it, moving what is above it – the earthquake. That is when vapor, smoke, wind, or something similar is generated under the earth, and the face of the earth is compact, without pores or with very narrow ones. It tries to escape but cannot due to the density of the earth, so it moves in itself and moves the earth, and perhaps splits it by its force. Burning fire may separate from it, and tremendous sounds due to intense friction and rubbing, and from it comes a roar due to the intensity of the wind. Earthquakes do not occur in soft lands due to the ease of exit, but one hears them in waves like mountains. Vapors. And rarely does it occur in summer due to the lack of compactness of the earth's surface. In lands where earthquakes are frequent, if many wells are dug in them so that the outlets for vapors increase, earthquakes decrease in them. There is no doubt that cold that occurs suddenly does what cold that occurs gradually does not.

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of earthly phenomena, which is earthquakes, and explains their physical cause. The author states that earthquakes occur when vapor, smoke, or similar substances become trapped under the earth. If the earth's surface is compact and lacks pores, the trapped substance cannot escape easily. It then moves and shakes the earth, possibly splitting it, and may produce fire and loud sounds. The author further explains that earthquakes are caused by the generation of vapor or wind underground. The earth's surface must be compact without pores. The trapped substance attempts to escape but cannot due to density, so it moves and shakes the ground. It may split the ground, and fire may come out due to friction. Loud sounds occur due to the intensity of movement. Earthquakes do not happen in soft, porous land because vapors can escape easily. The author then adds that earthquakes are rare in summer because the earth's surface is less compact. In areas with frequent earthquakes, digging wells to allow vapor to escape reduces their frequency. A solar eclipse can cause an earthquake because of sudden cold that traps winds underground. Sudden cold is more effective than gradual cold.

Causes of Springs, Wells, and Canals
Explains formation of springs, wells, and canals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how natural processes—particularly vapor turning into water and the presence of a continuous supply—lead to the formation of flowing springs, stagnant springs, wells, and canals.

Core Idea
Springs, wells, and canals are formed by natural cycles of vapor and water, not by magic.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explains the physical causes behind water sources. The author describes how underground vapors turn into water, and depending on abundance and continuous supply, they form flowing springs, stagnant pools, or wells and canals. The lesson shows that medieval scholars sought natural explanations for phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall explanation. Notice the key terms: vapor, continuous supply, springs, wells, canals.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the two main conditions
The passage describes two main factors: the abundance of vapor and the presence of a continuous supply (madad). Understand how these determine whether springs are flowing or stagnant.
3
Understand the vacuum cycle
The author explains that flowing springs continue due to a cycle: water flows out, creating a vacuum that attracts more vapor, which then turns into water. This is a natural explanation for perpetual flow.
This was a sophisticated medieval understanding of groundwater.
4
Connect to observable reality
The passage ends by linking the water sources to rain and snow, noting that they increase and decrease with rainfall. This grounds the explanation in empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وربما ينقلب البخار ماء فتنشق الأرض عيوناً جارية، إن كان لها مدد، وإلا فراكدة، وربما يفتقر إلى أن ينكشف عنه ثقل التراب، فيظهر آباراً وقنوات جارية، وللثلوج والأمطار أثر ظاهر في ذلك. إشارة إلى أسباب العيون والآبار والقنوات وذلك أن الأبخرة التي تحدث تحت الأرض إن كانت كثيرة، وانقلبت مياها انشق منها الأرض، فإن كان لها مدد حدث منها العيون الجارية، وتجري على الولاء لضرورة عدم الخلاء، فإنه كلما جرت تلك المياه انجذب إلى مواضعها هواء أو بخار آخر يتبرد بالبرد الحاصل هناك، فينقلب ماء أيضاً، وهكذا، إلا أنه يمنع مانع يحدث دفعة أو على التدريج، وإن لم يكن لتلك الأبخرة مدد حدثت العيون الراكدة، وإن لم تكن الأبخرة كثيرة بحيث تنشق الأرض، فإذا أزيل عن وجهها ثقل التراب، وصادفت منفذاً، واندفعت إليه حدث منها القنوات الجارية والآبار بحسب مصادفة المدد وفقدانه، وقد يكون سبب العيون والقنوات والآبار مياه الأمطار والثلوج. لأنـا نجدها تزيد بزيادتها وتنقص بنقصانها.
He said: And perhaps the vapor turns into water, so the earth splits into flowing springs – if it has a continuous supply, otherwise they are stagnant. Perhaps it needs to have the weight of soil removed from it, so wells and flowing canals appear. Snow and rain have a clear effect in that. Reference to the causes of springs, wells, and canals: The vapors that occur under the earth, if they are abundant and turn into water, the earth splits from them. If they have a continuous supply, flowing springs occur; they flow continuously due to the necessity of no vacuum. For whenever that water flows, air or another vapor is attracted to its place, cools by the cold present there, and turns into water again, and so on. However, a preventing factor may occur suddenly or gradually. If those vapors do not have a continuous supply, stagnant springs occur. If the vapors are not abundant enough to split the earth, then if the weight of soil is removed from its face, and it finds an outlet and flows to it, flowing canals and wells occur according to the presence or absence of a continuous supply. The cause of springs, canals, and wells may be rainwater and snow, for we find them increase with their increase and decrease with their decrease.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage explains the formation of springs, wells, and canals. The author describes how vapor under the earth can turn into water. If the vapor is abundant and has a continuous supply (مدد), it splits the earth and forms flowing springs. If there is no continuous supply, the water forms stagnant pools. If the vapor is less abundant and cannot split the earth on its own, removing the soil cover allows the water to come out as wells or canals. The author also notes that rain and snow contribute to these water sources, as they are observed to increase with rainfall and decrease with drought. The process involves a cycle: water flows, leaving a vacuum that attracts air or vapor, which then cools and turns into water again, ensuring continuous flow unless something blocks it. This explanation is based on natural observation and is presented as part of the discussion of earthly phenomena.

عيون (ʻuyūn): springs
آبار (ābār): wells
قنوات (qanawāt): canals
بخار (bukhar): vapor
مدد (madad): continuous supply

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That springs and wells are purely miraculous or caused by supernatural forces without natural explanation.
Correction: The passage shows that medieval scholars observed natural processes—vapor condensation, vacuum cycles, and rainfall—as the causes, offering a systematic natural explanation.

Philosophers' Causal Explanations for Earthquakes and Water Sources
Philosophers' causal explanations for earthquakes, springs, wells, and canals based on vapors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific causal mechanisms that philosophers proposed for earthquakes, springs, wells, and canals, based on vapors, condensation, and other natural processes.

Core Idea
Philosophers explain natural events through chains of physical causes like vapors and condensation.

Before dismissing the philosophers' views, it is important to understand them precisely. This passage presents their detailed causal explanations for earthquakes, springs, wells, and canals, all based on the behavior of subterranean vapors and temperature changes. Learning these helps us see why the kalam tradition ultimately found them insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the passage describing earthquakes and the formation of springs and wells. Note the technical terms.
The passage uses specific scientific terms for vapors, earthquakes, and water sources.
2
Identify the cause of earthquakes
Notice how the philosophers explain earthquakes as resulting from vapors and sudden cold, and why they are rare in summer.
The explanation involves both the condensation of the earth's surface and the confinement of winds.
3
Understand the formation of springs and wells
The passage describes how vapors turning into water can split the earth, creating flowing or stagnant springs. Wells and canals form when vapors are less abundant and soil is removed.
Note the role of a 'continuous supply' (madd) in distinguishing flowing springs from stagnant ones.
4
Recognize the role of rain and snow
The philosophers also acknowledge rain and snow as causes, since spring water increases with them.
This shows their attempt to correlate observable phenomena with their theories.

الأبخرة. وقلما يكون في الصيف لقلة تكاثف وجه الأرض، والبلاد التي يكثر فيها الزلزلة إذا حفرت فيها آبار كثيرة حتى كثرت مخالص الأبخرة، قلت الزلزلة بها، وقد يصير الكسوف سبباً للزلزلة لفقد الحرارة الكائنة عن الشعاع دفعة، وحصول البرد الحاقن للرياح في تجاويف الأرض بالتحصيل بغتة ولا شك أن البرد الذي يعرض بغتة يفعل ما لا يفعله العارض بالتدريج. قال وربما ينقلب البخار ماء فتنشق الأرض عيوناً جارية، إن كان لها مدد، وإلا فراكدة، وربما يفتقر إلى أن ينكشف عنه ثقل التراب، فيظهر آباراً وقنوات جارية، وللثلوج والأمطار أثر ظاهر في ذلك. إشارة إلى أسباب العيون والآبار والقنوات وذلك أن الأبخرة التي تحدث تحت الأرض إن هناك، فينقلب ماء أيضاً، وهكذا، إلا أنه يمنع مانع يحدث دفعة أو على التدريج، وإن لم يكن لتلك الأبخرة مدد حدثت العيون الراكدة، وإن لم تكن الأبخرة كثيرة بحيث تنشق الأرض، فإذا أزيل عن وجهها ثقل التراب، وصادفت منفذاً، واندفعت إليه حدث منها القنوات الجارية والآبار بحسب مصادفة المدد وفقدانه، وقد يكون سبب العيون والقنوات والآبار مياه الأمطار والثلوج لأنـا نجدها تزيد بزيادتها وتنقص بنقصانها. ثم إنهم يعترفون بأن ما ذكروا في الآثار العلوية والسفلية ظنون مبنية على حدس وتجربة وربما يصير يقيناً بالنسبة إلى بعض الأذهان لمعونة القرائن، وأنه لا يمتنع تكونها بأسباب أخر، وإن بعض ما ذكروا من الأسباب ناقص يفتقر إلى تأثير من القوى الروحانية، وفيما يشاهد في بلاد الترك من خواص النباتات والأحجار في أمر الرياح والأمطار ما يشهد بأن لا مؤثر إلا الله ولا خالق سواه. يعني أن ما ذكر في الآثار العلوية أي التي فوق الأرض والسفلية أي التي جميع على وجهها وتحتها، إنما هو رأي الفلاسفة لا المتكلمين القائلين باستناد ذلك إلى إرادة القادر المختار. ومع ذلك فالفلاسفة معترفون بأنها ظنون مبنية على حدس وتجربة يشاهد أمثالها كما يرى في الحمام من تصاعد الأبخرة وانعقادها، وتقاطرها، وفي البرد الشديد من تكاثف ما يخرج بالأنفاس كالثلج، وفي المرايا من اختلاف الصور والألوان، وانعكاس الأضواء على الأنحاء المختلفة، إلى غير ذلك، فهذا وأمثاله من التجارب والمشاهدات ما يفيد ظن استناد تلك الآثار إلى ما ذكروا من الأسباب، وقد ينضم إليها من قرائن الأحوال، ما يفيد اليقين الحدسي، ويختلف ذلك باختلاف الأشخاص فيحصل اليقين للبعض دون البعض. واعترفوا أيضاً بأنه لا يمتنع استنادها إلى أسباب أخر، لجواز أن يكون للواحد بالنوع علل متعددة، وأن يكون صدوره عن البعض أقلياً، وعن البعض أكثرياً. وبأن في جملة ما ذكر من الأسباب ما يحكم الحدس، بأنه غير البحر فيغوص فيه.
The vapors. And it is rare in summer due to lack of condensation of the earth's surface. And in lands where earthquakes are frequent, if many wells are dug in them until the outlets of vapors multiply, the earthquake decreases there. And an eclipse may become a cause of earthquake due to the loss of heat arising from radiation suddenly, and the occurrence of cold that confines the winds in the cavities of the earth by sudden contraction, and there is no doubt that cold which occurs suddenly does what cold that occurs gradually does not do. He said: And sometimes the vapor turns into water, so the earth splits open into flowing springs; if they have a continuous supply, otherwise stagnant. And sometimes it needs that the weight of soil be removed from it, so it appears as wells and flowing canals. And snow and rain have a clear effect in that. Reference to the causes of springs, wells, and canals: that the vapors that occur under the earth, if they are abundant and turn into water, the earth splits from them; if they have a continuous supply, flowing springs occur from them, and they flow continuously due to the necessity of no vacuum; for whenever that water flows, air or another vapor is drawn to its place, which is cooled by the cold present there, so it also turns into water, and so on, except that a hindrance prevents it, occurring suddenly or gradually. And if those vapors have no continuous supply, stagnant springs occur. And if the vapors are not abundant enough to split the earth, then if the weight of soil is removed from its surface, and it finds a passage and rushes to it, flowing canals and wells occur from them according to the occurrence or absence of a continuous supply. And the cause of springs, canals, and wells may be rainwater and snow, for we find them increase with their increase and decrease with their decrease. Then they acknowledge that what they mentioned about the celestial and terrestrial effects are conjectures based on intuition and experience, and perhaps they become certainty for some minds due to the assistance of indications, and that it is not impossible that they be caused by other causes, and that some of the causes they mentioned are deficient, needing the influence of spiritual forces. And in what is observed in the land of the Turks of the properties of plants and stones concerning wind and rain, there is evidence that there is no effecter but God and no creator besides Him. Meaning that what was mentioned of celestial effects, that is, those above the earth, and terrestrial effects, that is, those all on its surface and beneath it, is only the opinion of the philosophers, not of the mutakallimun who hold that this is dependent on the will of the Omnipotent and Free Agent. Nevertheless, the philosophers acknowledge that they are conjectures based on intuition and experience, examples of which are observed, as is seen in the bathhouse of the rising and condensation of vapors and their dripping, and in severe cold of the condensation of what comes out with breaths like snow, and in mirrors of the variation of images and colors and the reflection of lights in different directions, and so on. So these and similar experiments and observations yield a conjecture that those effects are dependent on the causes they mentioned. And there may join them circumstantial indications that yield intuitive certainty, and that varies according to persons, so certainty is obtained for some and not for others. And they also acknowledged that it is not impossible that they be dependent on other causes, because it is possible that a single species has multiple causes, and that its occurrence from some is less frequent and from others more frequent. And that among the causes they mentioned there are those about which intuition judges that they are incomplete in causality, but rather need the addition of spiritual forces; without them they would not be sufficient to necessitate what they are causes of. For among winds are those that uproot great trees and snatch ships from the seas, and among thunderbolts are those that strike a mountain and crush it, and strike the sea and plunge into it.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage serves a dual function: it first summarizes the philosophers' causal explanations for natural phenomena such as earthquakes, springs, wells, and canals, and then it presents their own admission that these explanations are merely conjectural, thereby reinforcing the kalam position that the only true agent is God. The passage begins by discussing earthquakes, stating that they are rare in summer due to the lack of condensation of the earth's surface, and that digging many wells reduces earthquakes by providing more outlets for vapors. It also notes that an eclipse can cause an earthquake because of the sudden loss of heat and the abrupt occurrence of cold that traps winds in the earth's cavities, emphasizing that a sudden cold has effects that gradual cold does not. Next, it presents a quoted statement (likely from the philosophers) explaining how vapor turning into water can split the earth, forming flowing or stagnant springs, and how the removal of soil can reveal wells and canals. It then elaborates on the process: abundant vapors turning into water cause the earth to split; if there is a continuous supply, flowing springs result, with water drawing in more air or vapor that itself turns into water, continuing until a hindrance stops it; if there is no supply, stagnant springs form; if vapors are not abundant, wells and canals appear after soil removal. Rain and snow are also cited as causes. Then the passage shifts to the philosophers' own acknowledgment (thumma innahum ya‘tarifūn) that all these explanations of celestial and terrestrial effects are mere conjectures (ẓunūn) based on intuition (ḥads) and experience (tajriba), though they may become certain for some minds with supporting indications (qarā’in). They further admit that it is not impossible for these effects to have other causes, and that some of the causes they mentioned are deficient, requiring the influence of spiritual forces (quwan rūḥāniyya). The text then cites observations in the land of the Turks as evidence that there is no effecter but God and no creator besides Him. The commentator (al-Taftazani) then explains that the entire discussion of celestial and terrestrial effects is the view of philosophers, not of the mutakallimun, who attribute everything to the will of the Omnipotent and Free Agent. The philosophers, however, themselves admit that their explanations are conjectural, providing examples from everyday experience (e.g., vapors in a bathhouse, condensation of breath in cold, reflections in mirrors) that yield only conjecture, not certain knowledge, though sometimes with circumstantial indications they can produce intuitive certainty for some individuals. They also acknowledge the possibility of multiple causes for a single effect and the incompleteness of some causes, which need spiritual forces—citing winds that uproot trees and thunderbolts that crush mountains as evidence that these forces are necessary. The entire argument underscores that natural causes are not independent agents; true causality belongs to God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Philosophers' Admission of Conjecture and the Kalam Conclusion
Philosophers' own admission that their causal explanations are conjectural, with kalam commentary affirming divine agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophers themselves admit their causal explanations are conjectural, and how this strengthens the kalam position that only God is the true cause.

Core Idea
Philosophers admit their theories are guesses; only God is the true agent.

After presenting the philosophers' detailed causal theories, the passage reveals a surprising turn: the philosophers themselves acknowledge that these explanations are mere conjectures based on intuition and experience. They even admit the possibility of other causes and the need for spiritual forces. This admission directly supports the kalam doctrine of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Begin from 'ثم إنهم يعترفون' and read to the end. Pay attention to how the philosophers qualify their own theories.
This section contains the kalam commentator's explanation as well.
2
Identify the philosophers' three acknowledgments
List the three admissions: (1) their explanations are conjectures, (2) other causes are possible, (3) some causes need spiritual forces.
The passage explicitly uses 'يعترفون' (they acknowledge).
3
Understand how certainty may arise
The passage says conjectures may become certainty for some minds due to circumstantial indications (qara'in). Reflect on why this still does not make them absolute.
Certainty here is intuitive, not demonstrative.
4
Connect to the kalam conclusion
The commentator explains that the entire discussion is the philosophers' view, and that the mutakallimun attribute all effects directly to God's will. The final statement 'لا مؤثر إلا الله' sums up the lesson.
This is the key takeaway for Islamic theology.

الأبخرة. وقلما يكون في الصيف لقلة تكاثف وجه الأرض، والبلاد التي يكثر فيها الزلزلة إذا حفرت فيها آبار كثيرة حتى كثرت مخالص الأبخرة، قلت الزلزلة بها، وقد يصير الكسوف سبباً للزلزلة لفقد الحرارة الكائنة عن الشعاع دفعة، وحصول البرد الحاقن للرياح في تجاويف الأرض بالتحصيل بغتة ولا شك أن البرد الذي يعرض بغتة يفعل ما لا يفعله العارض بالتدريج. قال وربما ينقلب البخار ماء فتنشق الأرض عيوناً جارية، إن كان لها
