وصادفت منفذاً، واندفعت إليه حدث منها القنوات الجارية والآبار بحسب مصادفة المدد وفقدانه، وقد يكون سبب العيون والقنوات والآبار مياه الأمطار والثلوج لأنـا نجدها تزيد بزيادتها وتنقص بنقصانها. ثم إنهم يعترفون بأن ما ذكروا في الآثار العلوية والسفلية ظنون مبنية على حدس وتجربة وربما يصير يقيناً بالنسبة إلى بعض الأذهان لمعونة القرائن، وأنه لا يمتنع تكونها بأسباب أخر، وإن بعض ما ذكروا من الأسباب ناقص يفتقر إلى تأثير من القوى الروحانية، وفيما يشاهد في بلاد الترك من خواص النباتات والأحجار في أمر الرياح والأمطار ما يشهد بأن لا مؤثر إلا الله ولا خالق سواه. يعني أن ما ذكر في الآثار العلوية أي التي فوق الأرض والسفلية أي التي جميع على وجهها وتحتها، إنما هو رأي الفلاسفة لا المتكلمين القائلين باستناد ذلك إلى إرادة القادر المختار. ومع ذلك فالفلاسفة معترفون بأنها ظنون مبنية على حدس وتجربة يشاهد أمثالها كما يرى في الحمام من تصاعد الأبخرة وانعقادها، وتقاطرها، وفي البرد الشديد من تكاثف ما يخرج بالأنفاس كالثلج، وفي المرايا من اختلاف الصور والألوان، وانعكاس الأضواء على الأنحاء المختلفة، إلى غير ذلك، فهذا وأمثاله من التجارب والمشاهدات ما يفيد ظن استناد تلك الآثار إلى ما ذكروا من الأسباب، وقد ينضم إليها من قرائن الأحوال، ما يفيد اليقين الحدسي، ويختلف ذلك باختلاف الأشخاص فيحصل اليقين للبعض دون البعض. واعترفوا أيضاً بأنه لا يمتنع استنادها إلى أسباب أخر، لجواز أن يكون للواحد بالنوع علل متعددة، وأن يكون صدوره عن البعض أقلياً، وعن البعض أكثرياً. وبأن في جملة ما ذكر من الأسباب ما يحكم الحدس، بأنه غير البحر فيغوص فيه.
The vapors. And it is rare in summer due to lack of condensation of the earth's surface. And in lands where earthquakes are frequent, if many wells are dug in them until the outlets of vapors multiply, the earthquake decreases there. And an eclipse may become a cause of earthquake due to the loss of heat arising from radiation suddenly, and the occurrence of cold that confines the winds in the cavities of the earth by sudden contraction, and there is no doubt that cold which occurs suddenly does what cold that occurs gradually does not do. He said: And sometimes the vapor turns into water, so the earth splits open into flowing springs; if they have a continuous supply, otherwise stagnant. And sometimes it needs that the weight of soil be removed from it, so it appears as wells and flowing canals. And snow and rain have a clear effect in that. Reference to the causes of springs, wells, and canals: that the vapors that occur under the earth, if they are abundant and turn into water, the earth splits from them; if they have a continuous supply, flowing springs occur from them, and they flow continuously due to the necessity of no vacuum; for whenever that water flows, air or another vapor is drawn to its place, which is cooled by the cold present there, so it also turns into water, and so on, except that a hindrance prevents it, occurring suddenly or gradually. And if those vapors have no continuous supply, stagnant springs occur. And if the vapors are not abundant enough to split the earth, then if the weight of soil is removed from its surface, and it finds a passage and rushes to it, flowing canals and wells occur from them according to the occurrence or absence of a continuous supply. And the cause of springs, canals, and wells may be rainwater and snow, for we find them increase with their increase and decrease with their decrease. Then they acknowledge that what they mentioned about the celestial and terrestrial effects are conjectures based on intuition and experience, and perhaps they become certainty for some minds due to the assistance of indications, and that it is not impossible that they be caused by other causes, and that some of the causes they mentioned are deficient, needing the influence of spiritual forces. And in what is observed in the land of the Turks of the properties of plants and stones concerning wind and rain, there is evidence that there is no effecter but God and no creator besides Him. Meaning that what was mentioned of celestial effects, that is, those above the earth, and terrestrial effects, that is, those all on its surface and beneath it, is only the opinion of the philosophers, not of the mutakallimun who hold that this is dependent on the will of the Omnipotent and Free Agent. Nevertheless, the philosophers acknowledge that they are conjectures based on intuition and experience, examples of which are observed, as is seen in the bathhouse of the rising and condensation of vapors and their dripping, and in severe cold of the condensation of what comes out with breaths like snow, and in mirrors of the variation of images and colors and the reflection of lights in different directions, and so on. So these and similar experiments and observations yield a conjecture that those effects are dependent on the causes they mentioned. And there may join them circumstantial indications that yield intuitive certainty, and that varies according to persons, so certainty is obtained for some and not for others. And they also acknowledged that it is not impossible that they be dependent on other causes, because it is possible that a single species has multiple causes, and that its occurrence from some is less frequent and from others more frequent. And that among the causes they mentioned there are those about which intuition judges that they are incomplete in causality, but rather need the addition of spiritual forces; without them they would not be sufficient to necessitate what they are causes of. For among winds are those that uproot great trees and snatch ships from the seas, and among thunderbolts are those that strike a mountain and crush it, and strike the sea and plunge into it.

Commentary
This passage serves a dual function: it first summarizes the philosophers' causal explanations for natural phenomena such as earthquakes, springs, wells, and canals, and then it presents their own admission that these explanations are merely conjectural, thereby reinforcing the kalam position that the only true agent is God. The passage begins by discussing earthquakes, stating that they are rare in summer due to the lack of condensation of the earth's surface, and that digging many wells reduces earthquakes by providing more outlets for vapors. It also notes that an eclipse can cause an earthquake because of the sudden loss of heat and the abrupt occurrence of cold that traps winds in the earth's cavities, emphasizing that a sudden cold has effects that gradual cold does not. Next, it presents a quoted statement (likely from the philosophers) explaining how vapor turning into water can split the earth, forming flowing or stagnant springs, and how the removal of soil can reveal wells and canals. It then elaborates on the process: abundant vapors turning into water cause the earth to split; if there is a continuous supply, flowing springs result, with water drawing in more air or vapor that itself turns into water, continuing until a hindrance stops it; if there is no supply, stagnant springs form; if vapors are not abundant, wells and canals appear after soil removal. Rain and snow are also cited as causes. Then the passage shifts to the philosophers' own acknowledgment (thumma innahum ya‘tarifūn) that all these explanations of celestial and terrestrial effects are mere conjectures (ẓunūn) based on intuition (ḥads) and experience (tajriba), though they may become certain for some minds with supporting indications (qarā’in). They further admit that it is not impossible for these effects to have other causes, and that some of the causes they mentioned are deficient, requiring the influence of spiritual forces (quwan rūḥāniyya). The text then cites observations in the land of the Turks as evidence that there is no effecter but God and no creator besides Him. The commentator (al-Taftazani) then explains that the entire discussion of celestial and terrestrial effects is the view of philosophers, not of the mutakallimun, who attribute everything to the will of the Omnipotent and Free Agent. The philosophers, however, themselves admit that their explanations are conjectural, providing examples from everyday experience (e.g., vapors in a bathhouse, condensation of breath in cold, reflections in mirrors) that yield only conjecture, not certain knowledge, though sometimes with circumstantial indications they can produce intuitive certainty for some individuals. They also acknowledge the possibility of multiple causes for a single effect and the incompleteness of some causes, which need spiritual forces—citing winds that uproot trees and thunderbolts that crush mountains as evidence that these forces are necessary. The entire argument underscores that natural causes are not independent agents; true causality belongs to God alone.

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans or rely on secondary causes in your daily life (e.g., a doctor's treatment, a job interview), do you truly feel in your heart that God is the only real agent, or do you functionally treat those causes as independent? What would change if you genuinely lived لا مؤثر إلا الله?
?
The philosophers admit that much of their knowledge is conjectural and uncertain. How much of what you confidently think you know about the world—your career, health, relationships—is actually built on mere conjecture? Are you willing to hold that knowledge loosely and anchor your heart in God alone?
?
If all causes are incomplete and need spiritual forces (or God's direct intervention) to be effective, then your successes and failures are ultimately from God. Think of a recent success or failure. Can you honestly say you acknowledged God's sole agency in that moment? What would it mean to live with that awareness starting today?

Philosophers' Explanations Are Conjectural, Not Certain
The author presents and then refutes the philosophers' view of natural causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' accounts of natural phenomena are considered conjectural and how they themselves admit the possibility of alternative causes.

Core Idea
Philosophers admit their natural explanations are only guesses, not certain truths.

After outlining the basic position, the author clarifies that the discussion of natural causes belongs to the philosophers, not to the theologians who affirm divine will. He then shows that even philosophers concede their explanations are conjectures based on intuition and everyday analogy, and that they accept the possibility of multiple causes for the same effect. This lesson unpacks those admissions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noticing the key admissions: that the explanations are conjectures, based on intuition and experience, and that they may become certain only for some.
Pay attention to the examples from bathhouse, frost, and mirrors.
2
Identify the distinction between philosophers and theologians
The author states that the view being discussed is that of the philosophers, not the theologians (المتكلمين), who attribute everything to God's will. Understand this contrast.
This sets up the refutation.
3
Understand why these explanations are only conjectural
The philosophers admit their accounts are ظنون (conjectures) built on حدس (intuition) and تجربة (experience). This yields probability, not certainty, and can vary among individuals.
Also note they allow for other possible causes.

And perhaps it becomes certainty for some minds with the help of indications, and that it is not impossible for them to be produced by other causes, and that some of the causes they mentioned are incomplete, needing influence from spiritual powers. And in what is observed in the lands of the Turks of the properties of plants and stones concerning wind and rain, there is witness that there is no effective agent except God and no creator other than Him. That is to say, what was mentioned about the upper effects (that is, those above the earth) and the lower effects (that is, those on its surface and beneath it) is only the opinion of the philosophers, not of the theologians, who hold that these are due to the will of the omnipotent Choser. Nevertheless, the philosophers admit that they are conjectures based on intuition and experience, similar to what is seen in the bathhouse of vapors rising, condensing, and dripping, and in severe cold of the congealing of what comes out with breath like snow, and in mirrors of the variation of images and colors and the reflection of lights in different ways, and so on. This and similar experiences and observations give a conjecture that those effects are due to the causes they mentioned, and there may be added to them indications from states that give intuitive certainty, and this varies according to persons, so certainty occurs for some but not others. And they also admit that it is not impossible for them to be due to other causes, because it is possible for a single effect of a kind to have multiple causes, and for its occurrence from some to be less frequent and from others more frequent. And that among the causes mentioned, intuition judges that it is incomplete in causality, but rather needs the joining of spiritual powers, without which they would not be sufficient to necessitate what they are causes of. For among the winds there is what uproots great trees and snatches ships from the seas, and among the thunderbolts there is what strikes a mountain and crushes it, and falls on the sea and sinks into it, and burns some of its creatures, and penetrates a porous thing without burning it, and melts what it encounters of dense solid bodies, even melting gold in a pouch without burning the pouch except what burns from the melting, and melts the boss of a shield without burning the shield. And among comets there are those that remain for several months, having longitudinal and latitudinal motions, and other strange matters in which the material and efficient causes mentioned are insufficient, but rather an influence from spiritual powers is necessary. And it is well-known in the lands of the Turks, and the regions of Ars and Bulghar, from the properties of plants and stones regarding clouds, winds, rain, and other things, what makes the mind certain that it is not produced by the plant or stone, but rather by the Creator of powers and ability. And I heard from more than one trustworthy person that when they travel in summer, they take along one of the disbelievers who uses some of those stones, supplicating and humbly addressing the Creator—glory be to Him and exalted—in their own way, and he has great discipline and abandonment of desires, and is traced to a particular group famous for bringing down rain. Then a cloud appears sufficient to shade those people, containing a wind that drives away mosquitoes from them, moving with them when they move and stopping when they stop.

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument rebutting the philosophers' claim that natural phenomena are produced by inherent material causes. The author begins by stating that such explanations can become certain for some minds through supporting indications, but acknowledges that the same effects could have other causes and that some proposed causes are incomplete, requiring spiritual powers. He then clarifies that the discussion of upper and lower phenomena (celestial and terrestrial) is the philosophers' view, not that of the theologians, who attribute everything to the will of God. Nevertheless, the philosophers themselves admit their accounts are only conjectures based on intuition and experience, similar to everyday observations like steam condensing in a bathhouse or frost forming from breath. These only yield probability, though sometimes combined with circumstantial evidence they can yield intuitive certainty for some individuals. The philosophers also concede the possibility of alternative causes, since one type of effect can have multiple causes with varying frequency. Moreover, some claimed causes are judged by intuition to be incomplete, needing the addition of spiritual powers. The author then gives examples: winds that uproot trees or snatch ships, lightning that crushes mountains or melts gold without burning the pouch—phenomena inexplicable by material and efficient causes alone. He further cites reports from Turkish regions about properties of plants and stones that clearly show the effects are not from the objects themselves but from God. He provides a specific anecdote: travelers bring a disbeliever who uses stones with supplication and ascetic practices to bring rain, resulting in a cloud that shades them and repels mosquitoes, moving with them. This demonstrates that the true agent is God, not natural objects.

The Inadequacy of Material Causes and the Need for Spiritual Powers
The author argues that many natural phenomena require spiritual powers beyond material causes, supported by examples and a reported miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why material and efficient causes alone are insufficient for explaining extraordinary natural phenomena and why spiritual powers are necessary, as argued by the author.

Core Idea
Some natural events require spiritual powers beyond mere matter and motion.

Having established that philosophers' explanations are conjectural, the author now points out that even they recognize some proposed causes are incomplete. He provides striking examples of natural phenomena that cannot be fully explained by material causes, such as winds that uproot trees and lightning that melts gold without burning its pouch. Finally, he presents an account from Turkish lands where plants and stones are used to summon rain, indicating direct divine intervention. This lesson examines these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and identify the main claim
Read the passage and find the statement that some causes are incomplete in causality (غير تام السببية) and need spiritual powers.
The claim is that material and efficient causes are insufficient.
2
Examine the examples of extraordinary phenomena
The author gives examples: winds that uproot trees and snatch ships, lightning that crushes mountains and melts gold without burning the pouch, and comets with complex motions. Notice how these exceed normal material causation.
Think about why such phenomena would require spiritual powers.
3
Reflect on the Turkish anecdote
The author reports a well-known practice in Turkish lands where using stones with supplication brings rain. This is presented as empirical evidence that the true agent is God, not the stones.
Consider how this anecdote supports the overall argument against purely material causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يصير يقيناً بالنسبة إلى بعض الأذهان لمعونة القرائن، وأنه لا يمتنع تكونها بأسباب أخر، وإن بعض ما ذكروا من الأسباب ناقص يفتقر إلى تأثير من القوى الروحانية، وفيما يشاهد في بلاد الترك من خواص النباتات والأحجار في أمر الرياح والأمطار ما يشهد بأن لا مؤثر إلا الله ولا خالق سواه. يعني أن ما ذكر في الآثار العلوية أي التي فوق الأرض والسفلية أي التي على وجهها وتحتها، إنما هو رأي الفلاسفة لا المتكلمين القائلين باستناد ذلك إلى إرادة القادر المختار. ومع ذلك فالفلاسفة معترفون بأنها ظنون مبنية على حدس وتجربة يشاهد أمثالها كما يرى في الحمام من تصاعد الأبخرة وانعقادها، وتقاطرها، وفي البرد الشديد من تكاثف ما يخرج بالأنفاس كالثلج، وفي المرايا من اختلاف الصور والألوان، وانعكاس الأضواء على الأنحاء المختلفة، إلى غير ذلك، فهذا وأمثاله من التجارب والمشاهدات ما يفيد ظن استناد تلك الآثار إلى ما ذكروا من الأسباب، وقد ينضم إليها من قرائن الأحوال، ما يفيد اليقين الحدسي، ويختلف ذلك باختلاف الأشخاص فيحصل اليقين للبعض دون البعض. واعترفوا أيضاً بأنه لا يمتنع استنادها إلى أسباب أخر، لجواز أن يكون للواحد بالنوع علل متعددة، وأن يكون صدوره عن البعض أقلياً، وعن البعض أكثرياً. وبأن في جملة ما ذكر من الأسباب ما يحكم الحدس، بأنه غير تام السببية، بل يفتقر إلى انضمام قوى روحانية لولاها لما كانت كافية في إيجاب ما هي أسبابه، فإن من الرياح ما يقلع الأشجار العظيمة ويختطف المراكب من البحار، وإن من الصواعق ما يقع على الجبل فيدكه، وعلى البحر فيغوص فيه، ويحرق بعض حيواناته، وما ينفذ في المتخلخل فلا يحرقه، ويذيب ما يصادفه من الأجسام الكثيفة الصلبة، حتى يذيب الذهب في الكيس، ولا يحرق الكيس إلا ما يحرق عن الذوب، ويذيب ضبة الترس ولا يحرق الترس، وإن من الكواكب ذوات الأذناب ما يبقى عدة شهور، ويكون لها حركات طولية وعرضية إلى غير ذلك من الأمور الغريبة التي لا يكفي فيها ما ذكر من الأسباب المادية والفاعلية، بل لا بد من تأثير من القوى الروحانية. وقد تواتر في بلاد الترك، ونواحي أرس وبلغار من خواص النباتات والأحجار في شأن السحب والرياح والأمطار وغير ذلك ما يجزم العقل بأنه ليس صادراً عن النبات والحجر، بل عن خالق القوى والقدر، وسمعت غير واحد من الثقاة، أنهم إذا سافروا في الصيف، اصطحبوا واحداً من الكفرة يقوم باستعمال بعض تلك الأحجار مبتهلاً متضرعاً في أثناء ذلك إلى الخالق سبحانه وتعالى على طريقتهم، وله رياضة عظيمة، وترك للشهوات، ونسب في جماعة مخصوصة مشهورة باستنزال المطر، فيحدث سحابة قدر ما يظل أولئك النفر، فيها ريح تدفع عنهم البعوض، تسير معهم إذا ساروا، وتقف إذا وقفوا.
And perhaps it becomes certainty for some minds with the help of indications, and that it is not impossible for them to be produced by other causes, and that some of the causes they mentioned are incomplete, needing influence from spiritual powers. And in what is observed in the lands of the Turks of the properties of plants and stones concerning wind and rain, there is witness that there is no effective agent except God and no creator other than Him. That is to say, what was mentioned about the upper effects (that is, those above the earth) and the lower effects (that is, those on its surface and beneath it) is only the opinion of the philosophers, not of the theologians, who hold that these are due to the will of the omnipotent Choser. Nevertheless, the philosophers admit that they are conjectures based on intuition and experience, similar to what is seen in the bathhouse of vapors rising, condensing, and dripping, and in severe cold of the congealing of what comes out with breath like snow, and in mirrors of the variation of images and colors and the reflection of lights in different ways, and so on. This and similar experiences and observations give a conjecture that those effects are due to the causes they mentioned, and there may be added to them indications from states that give intuitive certainty, and this varies according to persons, so certainty occurs for some but not others. And they also admit that it is not impossible for them to be due to other causes, because it is possible for a single effect of a kind to have multiple causes, and for its occurrence from some to be less frequent and from others more frequent. And that among the causes mentioned, intuition judges that it is incomplete in causality, but rather needs the joining of spiritual powers, without which they would not be sufficient to necessitate what they are causes of. For among the winds there is what uproots great trees and snatches ships from the seas, and among the thunderbolts there is what strikes a mountain and crushes it, and falls on the sea and sinks into it, and burns some of its creatures, and penetrates a porous thing without burning it, and melts what it encounters of dense solid bodies, even melting gold in a pouch without burning the pouch except what burns from the melting, and melts the boss of a shield without burning the shield. And among comets there are those that remain for several months, having longitudinal and latitudinal motions, and other strange matters in which the material and efficient causes mentioned are insufficient, but rather an influence from spiritual powers is necessary. And it is well-known in the lands of the Turks, and the regions of Ars and Bulghar, from the properties of plants and stones regarding clouds, winds, rain, and other things, what makes the mind certain that it is not produced by the plant or stone, but rather by the Creator of powers and ability. And I heard from more than one trustworthy person that when they travel in summer, they take along one of the disbelievers who uses some of those stones, supplicating and humbly addressing the Creator—glory be to Him and exalted—in their own way, and he has great discipline and abandonment of desires, and is traced to a particular group famous for bringing down rain. Then a cloud appears sufficient to shade those people, containing a wind that drives away mosquitoes from them, moving with them when they move and stopping when they stop.

Miracles as Divine Acts: Taftazani's Conclusion
Taftazani concludes the discussion on miracles, affirming divine attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's argument that miracles are directly caused by the Omnipotent Chooser and that denying them is like denying sensory reality.

Core Idea
Miracles are undeniable sensory realities directly from Allah.

Taftazani wraps up the discussion on miracles by emphasizing that the events described in sacred texts are as evident as sensory experiences. He provides examples and asserts that the Qur'anic and prophetic reports leave no doubt that these acts are from the Omnipotent Chooser. This lesson will walk through his concluding remarks and the theological stance they reinforce.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text carefully. Notice the examples Taftazani gives and how he links them to sensory denial.
The passage is dense; read it once before diving into translation.
2
Understand the examples
Identify the two miraculous events mentioned: a cloud and wind meeting a group, and a plant that opens iron fetters. Reflect on how these are presented as well-known.
3
Grasp the theological assertion
Taftazani states that denying these miracles is like denying sensory things. Then he affirms that scriptural texts are decisive in attributing such effects to al-Qādir al-Mukhtār (the Omnipotent Chooser).
This is the core of his argument against naturalistic explanations.
4
Reflect on the concluding Quranic allusion
The passage ends with a reference to Quran 24:40: 'but for whom God does not appoint light, he has no light.' Consider how this underscores the necessity of divine guidance to accept miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وترجع إذا رجعوا، وربما تستقبلهم فرقة أخرى معهم سحابة تكفيهم وريح إلى خلاف جهة هذه الريح وإنكار هذا عندهم من قبيل إنكار المحسوسات. وأما حديث النبات الذي ينفتح به القيد من الحديد على قوائم الفرس عند إصابته فمشهور. ولعمري إن النصوص الواردة في استناد أمثال هذه الآثار إلى القادر المختار قاطعة، وطرق الهدى إلى ذلك واضحة، لكن من لم يجعل الله له نوراً فما له من نور
And they return if they return, and perhaps another group will meet them with a cloud sufficient for them and a wind contrary to the direction of that wind, and denying this among them is like denying sensory things. As for the hadith of the plant by which the iron fetter on the horse's legs is opened when it strikes it, it is well-known. By my life, the texts regarding the attribution of such effects to the Omnipotent Chooser are decisive, and the paths of guidance to that are clear, but for whom God does not appoint light, he has no light.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage concludes a discussion on miracles, likely preceding the new section. Taftazani describes two miraculous events: a group being met by a cloud and wind, and a plant that opens iron fetters. He asserts that denying such miracles is like denying sensory perception. He then affirms that scriptural texts unequivocally attribute these effects to the Omnipotent Chooser (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār), and the path of guidance is clear. However, he ends with a Quranic allusion (24:40) that those whom God does not grant light remain in darkness. This serves as a strong affirmation of divine causation against naturalistic denials.

First Objection and Response on Mizaj Formation
Taftazani opens with an objection and two responses on elements’ role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial objection about the role of elements in mizaj and the response that elements are preparers, as well as the subsequent dialectical exchange about the breaking of qualities.

Core Idea
Elements are preparers, not agents; the breaking of intensity involves opposing qualities.

In this passage, Taftazani begins a series of objections and answers regarding how mizaj (temperament) is formed. The discussion focuses on whether the elements themselves actively produce the intermediate quality or merely prepare the matter. You will see the first major objection and its response, followed by further refinement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection is raised, then answered, then a counter-response is given.
This is typical of kalam dialectical reasoning.
2
Identify the two responses
The passage presents two linked arguments: first, that elements are mere preparers, and second, that the 'breaking' of a quality's intensity is caused by its opposite.
3
Understand the counter-response
After the second response, Taftazani gives a symmetric counter-argument, insisting that the intermediate quality must arise regardless, and then considers an alternative view.
This shows the thoroughness of the dialectic.
4
Ask: Why does this matter?
The debate sets the stage for Taftazani's own position. Understanding why the simple view fails helps you grasp his eventual solution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أجيب بأن المراد أن هذه معدات والمؤثر أمر مفارق موجباً كان ليكون الاعتداد للزوم أو مختاراً ليكون لمجرى العادة، أو بأن المنكسر سورة الكيفية وهو يحصل بنفس الكيفية المضادة كما في امتزاج الماء الشديد الحرارة بماء بارد بل فاتر بل أقل حرارة. أجيب بمثله في جانب الآخر مع القطع بحدوث الكيفية المتوسطة حيث لا صورة تقتضي انكسار سورة البرودة كما في امتزاج الماء الحار بالبارد. وإن التزم بأن ذلك ليس بالفعل والانفعال بل بزوال الكيفيتين وحدوث المتوسطة بسبب مفارق ألزم مثله في المزاج. وإن جعل التكاسر السورة النارية التي أحدثت الحرارة في الماء ظهر أن ليس يلزم كون الفاعل صور البسائط الممتزجة. والأشبه بأصولهم أنه صورة المائية بتوسط الحرارة العارضة. فإن صورة كل من العناصر تفعل في مادتها بالذات وفي غيرها بتوسط الكيفية ذاتية كانت أو عرضية فعلية أو انفعالية ومادته تنفعل كذلك. ولهذا لا يلزمهم ما يلزم القائلين بتفاعل الكيفيات أنفسها من انفعال الفعلية وفعل الانفعالية. فإن قيل: نحن نعلم قطعاً أن المنكسر عند الامتزاج هو مرارة النار وبرودة الماء مثلاً قلنا: نعم بمعنى أنها تنعدم وتحدث المتوسطة. وأما الذي يتأثر ويتغير من كيفية إلى كيفية فهو المادة لا غير. وكما لا يمتنع انفعالها في الكيفية الفعلية لا يمتنع فعل الصورة بالكيفية الانفعالية للقطع بأن صورة الماء مثلاً إنما تكسر يبوسة النار برطوبته لا ببرودته
For it might be answered that what is meant is that these are preparers and the effective agent is a separate thing, either necessitating to be for necessary existence or chosen to be according to habitual course, or that what is broken is the intensity of the quality and it comes about by the opposing quality itself, as in the mixing of very hot water with cold water, or even lukewarm or less hot. It is answered with the like of it on the other side, while being certain of the occurrence of the intermediate quality where no form requires the breaking of the intensity of cold, as in mixing hot water with cold. And if it is held that that is not by action and passion, but by the removal of the two qualities and the occurrence of the intermediate one by a separate cause, the like is necessary in mixture. And if the breaking is made to be the fiery form that caused heat in water, it becomes clear that it is not necessary that the agent be the forms of the simple mixed things. And the more fitting according to their principles is that it is the form of water through the intermediary of accidental heat. For the form of each element acts in its matter essentially, and in others through the intermediary of a quality, whether essential or accidental, active or passive, and its matter is similarly passive. And for this reason they are not subject to what the proponents of interaction of qualities themselves are subject to, namely the passivity of the active and the activity of the passive. For if it is said: We know with certainty that what is broken in mixture is the bitterness of fire and the coldness of water, for example, we say: Yes, in the sense that they cease and the intermediate arises. But that which is affected and changes from one quality to another is matter only. And just as its being passive in an active quality is not impossible, so the form's acting by a passive quality is not impossible, because it is certain that the form of water, for example, breaks the dryness of fire by its wetness, not by its coldness.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers regarding the mechanism of mizaj. Taftazani presents a possible answer: the elements are merely preparers, and the true agent is a separate entity (either necessary or habitual). Alternatively, the 'breaking' of a quality's intensity is caused by its opposite quality itself (e.g., hot water mixing with cold). He then responds with a counter-argument on the other side, emphasizing that the intermediate quality must occur regardless. If one insists it is not action and passion but removal and generation by a separate cause, the same applies to mizaj. He refines the account: the breaking is assigned to the fiery form that caused heat in water; this implies the agent need not be the forms of the simple elements. Rather, the water's form acts through accidental heat. He explains that each element's form acts in its own matter essentially and in others via a quality (essential or accidental, active or passive). Thus, they avoid the problem of passive qualities acting or active qualities being passive. An objector says: we know with certainty that what is broken is the quality (e.g., fire's bitterness, water's coldness). Taftazani agrees in the sense that the original qualities cease and the intermediate arises, but insists that only matter undergoes change from one quality to another. The form can act through a passive quality, as the form of water breaks fire's dryness by its wetness, not its coldness.

Key Terms
المزاج
المعدات
الكيفية
المنكسر
الفعل والانفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that elements directly cause the intermediate quality through literal interaction of qualities.
Correction: Taftazani points out that the elements are only preparers, and the true agent is separate; if qualities interact, it is by opposing intensities, not by themselves acting.

Checkpoint
According to the first response, what role do the elements play in mizaj?
A) They are the direct agents of change.
B) They are mere preparers.
C) They produce the intermediate quality on their own.
D) They are the forms that act.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows the first step in dismantling a naive view of mizaj: elements are not efficient causes; the real mechanism is more subtle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a mixture, like hot water mixing with cold, do you instinctively attribute the resulting warmth to the water's own power, or do you recognize it as a sign of Allah's direct creation? How does Taftazani's dismantling of inherent causality challenge your daily assumptions about cause and effect?
?
If you are certain that the intermediate quality arises without a necessary physical mechanism, does that certainty come from observation or from philosophical reasoning? And how does that affect your reliance on empirical evidence for theological truths?
?
Taftazani considers the possibility that the process involves removal of qualities and generation by a separate cause. Reflect on a situation in your life where you assumed a natural process was self-sufficient. How might you reframe that event as a direct act of Allah, and what difference would that make in your gratitude and reliance on Him?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
8 min
Taftazani's Refined Account of Mizaj
Taftazani constructs his own theory: forms use qualities as intermediaries, matter changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's refined account of mizaj, including how forms act through qualities and that only matter undergoes change.

Core Idea
Forms act through qualities; only matter changes, not the qualities themselves.

After the initial objections, Taftazani presents his own view. He assigns the 'breaking' to the fiery form that caused heat in water, and clarifies that the form of water acts through accidental heat. He then explains the general principle: each elemental form acts in its own matter essentially and in others via qualities, avoiding the problem of passive qualities acting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin with the full Arabic, then translation. Notice how Taftazani shifts from dialectic to constructive explanation.
2
Identify the key claim about the form of water
He says the more fitting view is that the form of water acts through accidental heat. Understand why this avoids problems.
This is the crux of his solution.
3
Understand the general principle
He states that each elemental form acts in its own matter essentially, and in others via a quality (essential or accidental, active or passive).
This is a metaphysical principle.
4
Grasp the clarification about matter
He stresses that only matter is affected and changes from one quality to another; the qualities themselves do not act or become passive.
5
Connect to the whole argument
See how this refined account answers the earlier objections and provides a consistent theory of mizaj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أجيب بأن المراد أن هذه معدات والمؤثر أمر مفارق موجباً كان ليكون الاعتداد للزوم أو مختاراً ليكون لمجرى العادة، أو بأن المنكسر سورة الكيفية وهو يحصل بنفس الكيفية المضادة كما في امتزاج الماء الشديد الحرارة بماء بارد بل فاتر بل أقل حرارة. أجيب بمثله في جانب الآخر مع القطع بحدوث الكيفية المتوسطة حيث لا صورة تقتضي انكسار سورة البرودة كما في امتزاج الماء الحار بالبارد. وإن التزم بأن ذلك ليس بالفعل والانفعال بل بزوال الكيفيتين وحدوث المتوسطة بسبب مفارق ألزم مثله في المزاج. وإن جعل التكاسر السورة النارية التي أحدثت الحرارة في الماء ظهر أن ليس يلزم كون الفاعل صور البسائط الممتزجة. والأشبه بأصولهم أنه صورة المائية بتوسط الحرارة العارضة. فإن صورة كل من العناصر تفعل في مادتها بالذات وفي غيرها بتوسط الكيفية ذاتية كانت أو عرضية فعلية أو انفعالية ومادته تنفعل كذلك. ولهذا لا يلزمهم ما يلزم القائلين بتفاعل الكيفيات أنفسها من انفعال الفعلية وفعل الانفعالية. فإن قيل: نحن نعلم قطعاً أن المنكسر عند الامتزاج هو مرارة النار وبرودة الماء مثلاً قلنا: نعم بمعنى أنها تنعدم وتحدث المتوسطة. وأما الذي يتأثر ويتغير من كيفية إلى كيفية فهو المادة لا غير. وكما لا يمتنع انفعالها في الكيفية الفعلية لا يمتنع فعل الصورة بالكيفية الانفعالية للقطع بأن صورة الماء مثلاً إنما تكسر يبوسة النار برطوبته لا ببرودته
For it might be answered that what is meant is that these are preparers and the effective agent is a separate thing, either necessitating to be for necessary existence or chosen to be according to habitual course, or that what is broken is the intensity of the quality and it comes about by the opposing quality itself, as in the mixing of very hot water with cold water, or even lukewarm or less hot. It is answered with the like of it on the other side, while being certain of the occurrence of the intermediate quality where no form requires the breaking of the intensity of cold, as in mixing hot water with cold. And if it is held that that is not by action and passion, but by the removal of the two qualities and the occurrence of the intermediate one by a separate cause, the like is necessary in mixture. And if the breaking is made to be the fiery form that caused heat in water, it becomes clear that it is not necessary that the agent be the forms of the simple mixed things. And the more fitting according to their principles is that it is the form of water through the intermediary of accidental heat. For the form of each element acts in its matter essentially, and in others through the intermediary of a quality, whether essential or accidental, active or passive, and its matter is similarly passive. And for this reason they are not subject to what the proponents of interaction of qualities themselves are subject to, namely the passivity of the active and the activity of the passive. For if it is said: We know with certainty that what is broken in mixture is the bitterness of fire and the coldness of water, for example, we say: Yes, in the sense that they cease and the intermediate arises. But that which is affected and changes from one quality to another is matter only. And just as its being passive in an active quality is not impossible, so the form's acting by a passive quality is not impossible, because it is certain that the form of water, for example, breaks the dryness of fire by its wetness, not by its coldness.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers regarding the mechanism of mizaj. Taftazani presents a possible answer: the elements are merely preparers, and the true agent is a separate entity (either necessary or habitual). Alternatively, the 'breaking' of a quality's intensity is caused by its opposite quality itself (e.g., hot water mixing with cold). He then responds with a counter-argument on the other side, emphasizing that the intermediate quality must occur regardless. If one insists it is not action and passion but removal and generation by a separate cause, the same applies to mizaj. He refines the account: the breaking is assigned to the fiery form that caused heat in water; this implies the agent need not be the forms of the simple elements. Rather, the water's form acts through accidental heat. He explains that each element's form acts in its own matter essentially and in others via a quality (essential or accidental, active or passive). Thus, they avoid the problem of passive qualities acting or active qualities being passive. An objector says: we know with certainty that what is broken is the quality (e.g., fire's bitterness, water's coldness). Taftazani agrees in the sense that the original qualities cease and the intermediate arises, but insists that only matter undergoes change from one quality to another. The form can act through a passive quality, as the form of water breaks fire's dryness by its wetness, not its coldness.

Key Terms
التكاسر
السورة النارية
الصورة المائية
الحرارة العارضة
الفعلية والانفعالية
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that qualities themselves interact and change, or that the form acts directly without intermediaries.
Correction: Taftazani insists that only matter changes; forms act through qualities as intermediaries, and the qualities themselves do not act or become passive.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's refined account, what is the role of accidental heat in the formation of mizaj?
A) It is the form itself.
B) It is an intermediary through which the form of water acts.
C) It is the matter that changes.
D) It is the quality that breaks the coldness.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's account explains mizaj by having forms act through qualities, with matter as the sole subject of change, avoiding the pitfalls of direct quality interaction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Taftazani teaches that true change is not in surface qualities but in the underlying matter. When you examine your own character, do you focus on changing outward behaviors (qualities) or do you seek a transformation of your very substance? What would it mean for your soul's 'matter' to be altered?
?
The passage argues that water breaks fire's dryness by wetness, not coldness. Is there a quality in your life that you have been using to 'break' a bad habit, but perhaps you have misidentified the effective quality? Have you been relying on willpower (coldness) when what is needed is gentleness (wetness)?
?
The proponents of direct quality interaction faced the problem of active qualities becoming passive. In your spiritual life, are you treating your virtues as fixed agents when they need to be flexible? Are you willing to let your activity become passivity before God when necessary? Commit to one area where you will let God change your inner substance, not just polish the surface.

6 min
What Breaks in Mixture? The Objection and Answer
The author answers an objection about whether qualities break in mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the objection that qualities break in mixture, and why he argues that only matter undergoes change.

Core Idea
In mixture, it is matter that changes, not the qualities themselves.

In this passage, al-Sanusi tackles a common objection against the theory of mixture (imtizaj). The objector assumes that we know for certain that qualities like heat and cold break in mixture. Al-Sanusi reinterprets what 'break' means, drawing a distinction between the qualities themselves and the underlying matter. This lesson unpacks his response and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objector's claim that we know with certainty that the 'breaker' in mixture is the heat of fire and coldness of water.
Notice the objector assumes qualities are what break.
2
Observe the author's affirmation
Al-Sanusi agrees but reinterprets: the original qualities cease and an intermediate quality occurs. This is what 'breaks' means.
He does not deny the phenomenon, only the interpretation.
3
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between qualities and matter. What is affected and changes is matter alone, not the qualities themselves.
Matter (al-madda) is the substrate that undergoes alteration.
4
Examine the parallel and proof
Al-Sanusi argues that just as matter can be affected in an active quality, so form can act through a passive quality. He gives the example of water breaking the dryness of fire by its moisture, not its coldness.
This shows that the action is through a passive quality (moisture) affecting matter.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The objector's assumption that qualities themselves break is rejected; instead, matter undergoes change, and the original qualities cease to exist as an intermediate arises.
This preserves the principle that qualities do not migrate but matter changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن نعلم قطعاً أن المنكسر عند الامتزاج هو مرارة [؟] النار وبرودة الماء مثلاً قلنا : نعم بمعنى أنها تنعدم وتحدث المتوسطة، وأما الذي يتأثر ويتغير من كيفية إلى كيفية، فهو المادة لا غير، وكما لا يمتنع انفعالها في الكيفية الفعلية لا يمتنع فعل الصورة بالكيفية الانفعالية للقطع بأن صورة الماء مثلاً، إنما تكسر يبوسة النار برطوبته لا ببرودته.
If it is said: We know with certainty that what breaks in mixture is the hotness of fire and the coldness of water, for example. We say: Yes, in the sense that they cease and the intermediate [quality] occurs. As for what is affected and changes from one quality to another, it is matter alone. And just as its being affected in an active quality is not impossible, so the form's acting through a passive quality is not impossible, because it is cut that the form of water, for example, breaks the dryness of fire by its moisture, not by its coldness.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning what happens to qualities in a mixture. The objector claims we know for certain that what breaks (al-munkasir) in mixture is, for example, the heat of fire and the coldness of water. The author answers by affirming this but reinterprets the meaning. He says that in one sense, the original qualities cease to exist and an intermediate quality arises. However, what undergoes affection and change from one quality to another is only the matter (al-madda), not the qualities themselves. Then he draws a parallel: just as matter can be affected in an active quality, it is not impossible for the form (sura) to act through a passive quality. As a proof, he gives the example of water's form breaking the dryness of fire by its moisture, not by its coldness. Thus, the objector's assumption that qualities themselves break is rejected; rather, the matter is what is affected.

Key Terms
المنكسر
الامتزاج
الكيفية المتوسطة
المادة
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think it is the qualities themselves that break and change in a mixture.
Correction: The author clarifies it is the matter that undergoes change; the original qualities cease to exist as an intermediate arises.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what changes in a mixture when a new intermediate quality arises?
A) The qualities themselves
B) The matter
C) The forms of the elements
D) Nothing changes

Key Takeaway
In mixture, only matter undergoes change; the original qualities cease to exist and an intermediate quality arises, while the forms act through passive qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a change in your spiritual state (e.g., from hardness of heart to softness), do you attribute that change to your own qualities being broken, or do you recognize that your very substance (your soul) is being acted upon by God's forms? How does this lesson reframe the way you view your own transformation?
?
The author says that matter alone is affected in mixture. In your life, when you undergo a trial that seems to 'break' you, do you focus on the temporary qualities (anger, grief) that cease, or do you realize that the core of your being (your heart) is the matter being changed? Will you allow the form of faith to act through passive qualities like patience?
?
If the qualities of fire and water cease in mixture to produce an intermediate, what qualities do you need to let go of so that God can produce a new, balanced character in you? Are you clinging to the heat of anger or the coldness of indifference? What step will you take today to surrender those qualities to God's transforming work?
